8. Sub-group breakdown (4 sub-groups, 22 terms assigned; counts below are distinct term-in-sub-group pairs and may sum higher than `terms assigned` when terms span multiple sub-groups):

| Sub-group | Label | Terms | Description |
|---|---|---:|---|
| `M46-A` | Inner closure | 14 | The inner-being condition of the person whose abundance has produced progressive closure toward God — the heart grown fat and unresponsive (sha.men), the trust redirected from God to material security, and the inner self-assessment anchored in material prosperity. This is not rebellion but satiation: the person who is full does not hunger; who feels secure does not seek; who trusts their wealth does not lean on God. The inner faculty of perception dulls; the inner faculty of trust misdirects; and the inner person becomes blind without knowing it. |
| `M46-B` | Hardness and insatiability | 7 | The inner-being condition of the person whose abundance has produced two related failures of the inner life — hardness toward others and insatiability within. Hardness: the capacity for pity (cha.mal) erodes; the wealthy person answers roughly, ignores the suffering neighbour, honors the powerful and dishonors the poor, acquires through violence. Insatiability: the eyes are never satisfied with riches; wealth possessed cannot be enjoyed; what was accumulated rots; the person toils endlessly but cannot ask for whom. Both express the same inner contraction — unable to give to others, unable to receive from what they have. |
| `M46-C` | The ordering of character and wealth | 7 | The inner-being observation of the wisdom tradition: character determines the quality and trajectory of material life. The soul of the diligent is richly supplied — inner character produces inner richness that includes but exceeds material wealth. The house of the righteous has genuine treasure; the income of the wicked brings trouble. Wisdom carries riches and righteousness together. The fear of the Lord is Zion's true treasure. And God governs the material domain entirely — he makes poor and makes rich. The inner person who knows this does not grasp for material goods but receives them as fruit and gift of right inner orientation. |
| `M46-D` | The inner life beyond material circumstance | 14 | The inner-being characteristics that emerge when the person is no longer governed by the wealth domain — three related but distinct postures. First, poverty of spirit: the inner condition of dependence and receptivity before God, the person who has nothing to offer and is therefore constitutively open to divine gift; God has chosen the ptōchos of this world to be rich in faith. Second, the inner act of release: the deliberate choice to give, relinquish, or refuse material wealth in response to a higher valuation — Moses counting the reproach of Christ as greater wealth than Egypt's treasure; Abram refusing enrichment to preserve his witness; the widow giving all her living; Christ becoming poor so that others might become rich. Third, learned contentment: the inner stability that Paul names as acquired through living in both abundance and scarcity — an achieved inner independence from material circumstance that neither poverty destroys nor abundance corrupts. |

<a id="s2"></a>
## §2. Verses by sub-group

All verses for cluster terms, grouped by the analytical sub-group the term belongs to. Within each sub-group the rows are sorted canonically (book · chapter · verse). The **Term** column shows the Strong's number and transliteration of the cluster term whose `wa_verse_records` row generated this entry; **Spans in verse** lists every term-span recorded at that verse location.

Status precedence: G = group-assigned (analysed) · SA = set-aside · NR = is_relevant=0 · P = pending in VC · UT = untouched (no VC row).

**ID columns:** `vr_id` is `wa_verse_records.id`; `mti_id` is `mti_terms.id` (also `wa_verse_records.mti_term_id` and `verse_context.mti_term_id`). Both are exposed so AI can author VCREVISE / VCNEW / VCVERSE patches directly from this report — no separate ID-resolver query needed. The `(vr_id, mti_id)` pair is the natural key for any `verse_context` operation.

<a id="s2-1"></a>
### §2.1 `M46-A` — Inner closure

_The inner-being condition of the person whose abundance has produced progressive closure toward God — the heart grown fat and unresponsive (sha.men), the trust redirected from God to material security, and the inner self-assessment anchored in material prosperity. This is not rebellion but satiation: the person who is full does not hunger; who feels secure does not seek; who trusts their wealth does not lean on God. The inner faculty of perception dulls; the inner faculty of trust misdirects; and the inner person becomes blind without knowing it._

1. Terms in sub-group (14): H1878 da.shen, H7600 sha.a.nan, H7230 rov, H8082 sha.men, H4924B mish.man, H8080 sha.men, H1952 hon, G4145 plousios, G4147 plouteō, G4149 ploutos, G4434 ptōchos, H6223 a.shir, H6238 a.shar, H6239 o.sher
2. Verse-context groups in this sub-group (10):

| Group code | Term | Description | Verses (here) |
|---|---|---|---:|
| `1142-001` | H1952 *hon* | Term names abundance or greatness as a scalar applied to inner-moral realities — the weight or magnitude of iniquity (Jer 13:22; 30:14-15; Lam 1:5), the abundance of steadfast love that answers it (Lam 3:32; Isa 63:7; Neh 13:22), the greatness of spiritual failure (Hos 9:7), and the magnitude of false spiritual resources that cannot save (Isa 47:9-13). Rov does not name wealth in itself but names the weight of what accumulates in the inner-moral domain — and how the magnitude of divine faithfulness exceeds the magnitude of human failure. Anchor: Isa 63:7 (abundance of his steadfast love); Hos 9:7 (great iniquity and great hatred). | 28 |
| `7577-001` | G4434 *ptōchos* | Term names the inner-closure condition of the self-assessed wealthy person — the one who says 'I am rich, I need nothing' and thereby closes themselves to receiving from God. Wealth has become both the self-assessment and the perceived need-eliminator. The Laodicean church (Rev 3:17) is the paradigm: materially self-sufficient, spiritually blind and wretched without knowing it. Luk 6:24 names the same pattern from another angle: the rich have received their consolation — their inner satisfaction is fully met in this age, leaving nothing for the age to come. Mat 19:23-24 names the inner obstacle wealth creates for kingdom entry. Anchor: Rev 3:17. | 17 |
| `7579-001` | H1952 *hon* | Term names the self-directed drive to become rich as the inner-being condition that produces closure and ruin — those who desire to be rich fall into temptation, snare, and many harmful desires that plunge them into destruction (1Ti 6:9); the Corinthians' premature self-assessment of inner richness and kingly status (1Cor 4:8 — ironic); the Laodicean church's claimed prosperity as the ground of inner self-sufficiency (Rev 3:17); merchants who grew rich through Babylon's exploitation and mourn its loss (Rev 18:3; 15; 19). The self-directed orientation toward becoming rich is identified as the inner problem before any wealth exists (1Ti 6:9 — desire to be rich). Anchor: 1Ti 6:9. | 9 |
| `7585-001` | H6238 *a.shar* | Term names the inner drive to become rich as the source of moral failure and self-deception — Ephraim declares 'I am rich; I have found wealth for myself; they cannot find iniquity in me' (Hos 12:8): the self-declaration of wealth as the ground of self-assessed innocence; corrupt shepherds thank God for their enrichment while exploiting the flock (Zec 11:5); hastening to be rich brings punishment (Pro 28:20); the wicked will not become rich (Job 15:29). The inner motivation to accumulate — when it is self-directed and operates through exploitation or self-deception — produces the inner-closure pattern of M46-A. Anchor: Hos 12:8. | 9 |
| `413-001` | H7600 *sha.a.nan* | Term names the inner condition of complacent ease that wealth and security produce — the settled, untroubled self-assurance of those whose material and social position has made them feel unthreatened. This ease is not the shalom of God's gift but a self-generated security that produces contempt for those in misfortune, blindness to coming judgment, and arrogance toward God. In Job 12:5 it produces contempt; in Amos 6:1 it receives a prophetic woe; in 2Ki 19:28 it is the inner ground of Assyrian rage against God. Anchor: Amo 6:1. | 7 |
| `4697-002` | H1878 *da.shen* | Term names the spiritual fatness that material prosperity produces — the person who has grown fat through abundance forsakes the God who made them and scoffs at the Rock of their salvation. The sequence is evidenced in Deu 32:15, Neh 9:25, and Jer 5:28: material provision received → satiation → fat → turned from God / unbounded in wickedness / justice abandoned. Growing fat is the inner condition that removes the constraint on sin and the orientation toward God. Anchor: Deu 32:15. | 4 |
| `4695-001` | H8082 *sha.men* | Term names material richness as the ambient condition within which the inner person's orientation toward God is tested and typically found wanting — the rich land God gave Israel became the context in which they ate, grew fat, and turned from him (Neh 9:25; 9:35); the rich food and luxury of the Chaldean conqueror is the fruit of self-attributed military success that produces a worship of one's own means (Hab 1:16). Eschatological rich bread (Isa 30:23) stands as the divinely-given form of sha.men provision in which the ordered inner response is gratitude. Anchor: Neh 9:35. | 3 |
| `4696-001` | H4924B *mish.man* | Term names the material fatness and robustness of accumulated human power as the condition that divine judgment reduces — the stout warriors of Assyria struck with wasting sickness by the Lord of hosts (Isa 10:16); the fat flesh of Jacob's former glory growing lean in judgment (Isa 17:4). The mish.man condition — the thick, robust, well-provisioned state — is here specifically what is stripped away. Material prosperity and physical abundance are the outer markers of the condition that divine judgment addresses at its root. Anchor: Isa 10:16. | 2 |
| `4697-001` | H8080 *sha.men* | Term names the making-fat of the inner heart as the mechanism of divine judgment — God causing the heart to grow thick and unresponsive so that the person can no longer see, hear, or understand, and therefore cannot turn and be healed. The same vocabulary that describes material prosperity (fatness) is applied here to the inner organ of perception: the fat heart is the closed heart. Anchor: Isa 6:10. | 1 |
| `7586-001` | H1952 *hon* | Term names material riches as the object of misplaced trust and the condition of insatiability — those who trust in their wealth and boast of the abundance of their riches (Psa 49:6); the man who trusted in the abundance of his riches rather than making God his refuge (Psa 52:7); whoever trusts in riches will fall (Pro 11:28); the eyes never satisfied with riches (Ecc 4:8); wealth present but the power to enjoy it absent (Ecc 6:2); riches kept to the owner's hurt and then lost (Ecc 5:13-14); Haman recounting the splendour of his riches to his household (Est 5:11). Material wealth presented as the inner security foundation and found to be unreliable, insatiable, and ultimately alienating. Anchor: Psa 52:7. | 1 |

<a id="s2-2"></a>
### §2.2 `M46-B` — Hardness and insatiability

_The inner-being condition of the person whose abundance has produced two related failures of the inner life — hardness toward others and insatiability within. Hardness: the capacity for pity (cha.mal) erodes; the wealthy person answers roughly, ignores the suffering neighbour, honors the powerful and dishonors the poor, acquires through violence. Insatiability: the eyes are never satisfied with riches; wealth possessed cannot be enjoyed; what was accumulated rots; the person toils endlessly but cannot ask for whom. Both express the same inner contraction — unable to give to others, unable to receive from what they have._

1. Terms in sub-group (7): G3045 liparos, H1952 hon, G4145 plousios, G4149 ploutos, G4434 ptōchos, H6223 a.shir, H6239 o.sher
2. Verse-context groups in this sub-group (7):

| Group code | Term | Description | Verses (here) |
|---|---|---|---:|
| `7586-001` | H1952 *hon* | Term names material riches as the object of misplaced trust and the condition of insatiability — those who trust in their wealth and boast of the abundance of their riches (Psa 49:6); the man who trusted in the abundance of his riches rather than making God his refuge (Psa 52:7); whoever trusts in riches will fall (Pro 11:28); the eyes never satisfied with riches (Ecc 4:8); wealth present but the power to enjoy it absent (Ecc 6:2); riches kept to the owner's hurt and then lost (Ecc 5:13-14); Haman recounting the splendour of his riches to his household (Est 5:11). Material wealth presented as the inner security foundation and found to be unreliable, insatiable, and ultimately alienating. Anchor: Psa 52:7. | 13 |
| `7010-001` | H6223 *a.shir* | Term names material wealth in its relation to the inner life of the person who holds it — its capacity to function as an imagined stronghold (Pro 18:11 — high wall in his imagination), to distort relationship (Pro 19:4 — wealth brings many friends; poor man deserted), to produce insatiability (Pro 30:15 — never says enough), and to reveal the inner person's character in how it is gained and used. Hon in its wisdom-ordered forms (Pro 3:9 — honoring God with wealth; Pro 8:18 — enduring wealth with righteousness; Song 8:7 — cannot purchase love) routes to M46-C. This VCG holds the verses where hon names wealth as the inner-being pressure point. Anchor: Pro 18:11 (imagined stronghold); Pro 30:15 (insatiability). | 12 |
| `7584-001` | H6239 *o.sher* | Term names the inner condition of the wealthy person whose material abundance has produced specific distortions — wise in their own eyes (Pro 28:11); unable to sleep from the restlessness of abundance (Ecc 5:12); answering roughly those who petition (Pro 18:23); exercising structural dominion over the poor (Pro 22:7); full of violence (Mic 6:12); unwilling to give from their own abundance, taking instead from the poor (2Sa 12:4); ignoring the suffering neighbor while feasting daily (Luk 16:19-21); going to bed rich and waking to find wealth gone (Job 27:19). Wealth produces a specific inner posture toward others and toward the person's own condition. Anchor: Pro 28:11. | 12 |
| `7577-002` | G4145 *plousios* | Term names the wealthy person whose abundance has produced hardness toward others — the rich man who feasts daily while Lazarus desires scraps at his gate (Luk 16:19-21); the rich who oppress and drag the poor into court (Jam 2:6); the rich who weep and howl at the judgment of their unjustly accumulated wealth (Jam 5:1); the assembly that honors the wealthy and dishonors the poor (Jam 2:2-3). The inner capacity for pity and genuine relationship has been overridden by the social power differential wealth creates. Anchor: Luk 16:19. | 7 |
| `7583-002` | G4434 *ptōchos* | Term names the poor person as the moral test of the wealthy person's inner condition — the assembly that pays attention to the rich visitor and tells the poor man to stand reveals what is in the heart (Jam 2:2-3); the community that dishonors the poor while honoring the rich has made a moral judgment about persons based on material condition; the false concern of Judas for the poor (Joh 12:6) reveals that invoking the poor can itself be an inner act of concealment. How a person treats the ptōchos is the index of their inner moral condition. Anchor: Jam 2:2-3. | 5 |
| `7581-001` | G4149 *ploutos* | Term names material riches as the choking and deceiving inner force — the deceitfulness (apatē) of riches competes with the word of God for the inner person's receptivity and produces fruitlessness (Mat 13:22; Mar 4:19; Luk 8:14); riches that have been unjustly accumulated rot and are moth-eaten (Jam 5:2); the person who has riches set their hope on something inherently uncertain (1Ti 6:17). The word apatē names the mechanism: riches present themselves as satisfying and securing but they are deceptive — they do not deliver what they promise, they choke rather than nourish, they decay rather than endure. Anchor: Mat 13:22. | 4 |
| `4702-001` | G3045 *liparos* | Term names the luxurious goods of Babylon as the object of the soul's longing — 'your soul longed for all your delicacies (liparos) and your splendors, lost to you never to be found again' (Rev 18:14). The soul (psuchē) oriented toward material luxury names the insatiability and deceitfulness face of the wealth domain: the inner person whose desire was attached to the richness of Babylon mourns its loss. Single-verse term; anchor set for R4 compliance. | 1 |

<a id="s2-3"></a>
### §2.3 `M46-C` — The ordering of character and wealth

_The inner-being observation of the wisdom tradition: character determines the quality and trajectory of material life. The soul of the diligent is richly supplied — inner character produces inner richness that includes but exceeds material wealth. The house of the righteous has genuine treasure; the income of the wicked brings trouble. Wisdom carries riches and righteousness together. The fear of the Lord is Zion's true treasure. And God governs the material domain entirely — he makes poor and makes rich. The inner person who knows this does not grasp for material goods but receives them as fruit and gift of right inner orientation._

1. Terms in sub-group (7): H1878 da.shen, H2633 cho.sen, H8082 sha.men, H1952 hon, H6223 a.shir, H6238 a.shar, H6239 o.sher
2. Verse-context groups in this sub-group (6):

| Group code | Term | Description | Verses (here) |
|---|---|---|---:|
| `7586-002` | H8082 *sha.men* | Term names material riches as the gift and fruit of the ordered inner life — Wisdom carries riches in her left hand alongside long life in her right (Pro 3:16); riches and honor are with Wisdom, enduring wealth and righteousness (Pro 8:18); the reward of humility and fear of the Lord is riches and honor and life (Pro 22:4); wealth and riches are in the house of the righteous, paired with enduring righteousness (Psa 112:3). Solomon chose wisdom rather than riches and received riches as the unasked-for consequence (1Ki 3:11-13; 2Ch 1:11-12). Both riches and honor come from God who governs all (1Ch 29:12). Anchor: Pro 8:18. | 16 |
| `7585-002` | H1952 *hon* | Term names enrichment as the fruit of diligence and divine blessing — the hand of the diligent makes rich (Pro 10:4); the blessing of the Lord makes rich, and he adds no sorrow with it (Pro 10:22); the Lord makes poor and makes rich, brings low and exalts (1Sa 2:7). Inner character (diligence) produces material enrichment; divine blessing produces material enrichment without the hidden cost that self-generated accumulation carries. The same verb names enrichment as earned through character and as given through divine sovereignty. Anchor: Pro 10:22. | 6 |
| `681-001` | H1952 *hon* | Term names wealth and treasure as the moral category in which inner character is revealed — in the house of the righteous there is much treasure; trouble befalls the income of the wicked. The character of the person shapes the moral quality of what they hold. Cho.sen also names the true treasure: the fear of the Lord is Zion's cho.sen — the inner realities of salvation, wisdom, and knowledge are the real wealth, which material cho.sen only shadows. Wealth obtained through violence (Eze 22:25) and wealth that does not last (Pro 27:24) complete the picture. Anchor: Isa 33:6. | 5 |
| `111-001` | H1878 *da.shen* | Term names the inner enrichment or richly-supplied condition of the soul that flows from right inner character — the soul of the diligent is richly supplied (Pro 13:4; ne.phesh + da.shen is the only instance in the cluster where prosperity vocabulary is applied directly to the ne.phesh); whoever brings blessing will be enriched (Pro 11:25); the one who trusts in the Lord will be enriched (Pro 28:25). Good news refreshes the bones (Pro 15:30 — da.shen applied to inner-physical renewal through received blessing). Character (diligence, generosity, trust) is the inner condition that produces material and inner enrichment as its fruit. Anchor: Pro 13:4. | 3 |
| `7584-002` | H6223 *a.shir* | Term names the rich person in the wisdom tradition's moral examination — the rich and poor stand equally before God who made them both (Pro 22:2); better is the poor person who walks in integrity than the rich person who is crooked in their ways (Pro 28:6); the rich man unwilling to give from his own flock reveals a specific inner moral failure (2Sa 12:4 — moral lesson context). The wisdom tradition consistently evaluates the a.shir not by their material condition but by their inner character — and regularly finds that integrity in poverty exceeds wealth in crookedness. Anchor: Pro 22:2. | 3 |
| `7584-001` | H6239 *o.sher* | Term names the inner condition of the wealthy person whose material abundance has produced specific distortions — wise in their own eyes (Pro 28:11); unable to sleep from the restlessness of abundance (Ecc 5:12); answering roughly those who petition (Pro 18:23); exercising structural dominion over the poor (Pro 22:7); full of violence (Mic 6:12); unwilling to give from their own abundance, taking instead from the poor (2Sa 12:4); ignoring the suffering neighbor while feasting daily (Luk 16:19-21); going to bed rich and waking to find wealth gone (Job 27:19). Wealth produces a specific inner posture toward others and toward the person's own condition. Anchor: Pro 28:11. | 1 |

<a id="s2-4"></a>
### §2.4 `M46-D` — The inner life beyond material circumstance

_The inner-being characteristics that emerge when the person is no longer governed by the wealth domain — three related but distinct postures. First, poverty of spirit: the inner condition of dependence and receptivity before God, the person who has nothing to offer and is therefore constitutively open to divine gift; God has chosen the ptōchos of this world to be rich in faith. Second, the inner act of release: the deliberate choice to give, relinquish, or refuse material wealth in response to a higher valuation — Moses counting the reproach of Christ as greater wealth than Egypt's treasure; Abram refusing enrichment to preserve his witness; the widow giving all her living; Christ becoming poor so that others might become rich. Third, learned contentment: the inner stability that Paul names as acquired through living in both abundance and scarcity — an achieved inner independence from material circumstance that neither poverty destroys nor abundance corrupts._

1. Terms in sub-group (14): H1878 da.shen, H7600 sha.a.nan, H5727 a.dan, G0842 autarkēs, H1952 hon, H2630 cha.san, G4145 plousios, G4146 plousiōs, G4147 plouteō, G4148 ploutizō, G4149 ploutos, G4433 ptōcheuō, G4434 ptōchos, H6238 a.shar
2. Verse-context groups in this sub-group (13):

| Group code | Term | Description | Verses (here) |
|---|---|---|---:|
| `7583-001` | G4434 *ptōchos* | Term names poverty as the inner posture of dependence and openness before God — the condition in which the person has nothing to offer and is therefore constitutively receptive to divine gift. The poor in spirit (Mat 5:3) are not people lacking in spirit but those who know they have no inner resource to bring; the kingdom belongs to them. The materially poor are the primary recipients of the gospel (Luk 4:18; 4:20) and the heirs of the kingdom (Luk 6:20; Jam 2:5). God has specifically chosen the ptōchos of this world to be rich in faith. Material poverty may be the condition that most naturally removes the inner buffer of self-sufficiency and creates openness. Anchor: Mat 5:3. | 9 |
| `7583-003` | H1878 *da.shen* | Term names voluntary giving from poverty and the command to give to the poor as acts that produce genuine enrichment and kingdom treasure. The widow who gave all her living gave more than all the wealthy (Luk 21:3; Mar 12:43) — her gift was total and was evaluated by Jesus as greater. The commands to give to the poor (Luk 18:22; Mat 19:21) are the specific acts of release by which kingdom treasure is obtained. Zacchaeus giving half to the poor is the spontaneous inner-being response to encounter with Jesus (Luk 19:8). The giving to the poor is both the external act and the inner act of relinquishing the attachment that wealth creates. Anchor: Luk 21:3. | 8 |
| `7579-002` | G4147 *plouteō* | Term names becoming rich in its directed-toward-God and toward-others form — not laying up treasure for oneself but being rich toward God (Luk 12:21); being rich in good works and generous (1Ti 6:18); enriched through Christ's poverty so that others might become rich (2Cor 8:9); God bestowing his riches on all who call on him (Rom 10:12); the counsel to buy from Christ gold refined by fire to be truly rich (Rev 3:18). The same verb that names the self-directed wealth-orientation (7579-001) here names the ordered orientation: toward God, toward others, through encounter with Christ. Anchor: Luk 12:21 (rich toward God). | 4 |
| `7577-003` | G4145 *plousios* | Term names wealth in its Christological inversion and eschatological revaluation — he who was rich became poor for our sake (2Cor 8:9); the materially poor Smyrna church is rich before God (Rev 2:9); God himself is rich in mercy (Eph 2:4); the poor who are rich in faith are the ones God has chosen (Jam 2:5). The plousios vocabulary is here deployed in its reversed form: the true wealth is not material, and the materially poor may be the genuinely rich. Anchor: Rev 2:9. | 3 |
| `7580-001` | G4434 *ptōchos* | Term names the enrichment of others as the inner-being goal and outcome of apostolic poverty — as poor yet making many rich (2Cor 6:10); enriched in every way to be generous in every way (2Cor 9:11). The through-put pattern: material poverty is the condition from which relational and spiritual enrichment of others flows. The person who is enriched (ploutizō) by God is enriched not as the terminal destination but as the instrument of further generosity. Anchor: 2Cor 6:10. | 3 |
| `7578-001` | H1952 *hon* | Term names the abundance of divine provision — God who richly provides all things for enjoyment (1Ti 6:17). The adverb plousiōs applied to God's giving names the quality of divine generosity: it is not minimal or grudging but rich and abundant. This is the proper ground of the ordered inner life — not riches themselves but the God who gives richly. The person who anchors hope in God rather than in uncertain riches finds that the alternative ground is itself characterized by plousiōs generosity. Anchor: 1Ti 6:17. | 2 |
| `7581-002` | G4149 *ploutos* | Term names riches in their revaluation by a higher inner-being calculus — the wealth of generosity that flows from poverty and overflowing joy (2Cor 8:2); the reproach of Christ counted as greater wealth than the treasures of Egypt because Moses was looking to the reward (Heb 11:26). In both cases the person's inner vision — what they are looking toward, what they count as truly valuable — has reordered the wealth calculus. The ploutos of genuine generosity exceeds material ploutos; the ploutos of Christ's reproach exceeds the treasures of Egypt. Anchor: Heb 11:26. | 2 |
| `7585-003` | H6238 *a.shar* | Term names the deliberate refusal of enrichment as an act of inner integrity and wisdom — Abram refuses to take a thread or sandal strap from the king of Sodom lest the king claim to have made him rich (Gen 14:23): the inner act of not-being-enriched to preserve the testimony of dependence on God alone; wisdom counsels do not toil to acquire wealth, be discerning enough to desist (Pro 23:4). The inner discipline of restraining the wealth-seeking drive is itself an inner-being act of ordering the inner life beyond material circumstance. Anchor: Gen 14:23. | 2 |
| `4898-001` | G0842 *autarkēs* | Term names contentment as a learned inner disposition — the acquired capacity of the inner person to remain settled and sufficient regardless of external material circumstance. Paul has learned this through living in both abundance and scarcity; it is not a natural state but an achieved one. The person who is autarkēs is no longer governed by the wealth domain in either direction — neither destroyed by poverty nor corrupted by abundance. This is the capstone inner-being state of M46-D: the inner life that has been formed beyond material circumstance. Anchor: Phil 4:11. | 1 |
| `3836-001` | H5727 *a.dan* | Term names the inner act of luxuriating in or delighting in received abundance — specifically delighting in God's goodness as the source. Neh 9:25: they ate, were filled, became fat, and delighted (a.dan) in your great goodness. This is the ordered face of abundance received with joy and conscious orientation toward the Giver. The same verse that documents the prosperity-apostasy pattern shows a.dan as the inner act that preceded the turning — delight in goodness, if not tethered to the Giver, becomes the ground of forgetting. In its ordered form a.dan names the legitimate inner enjoyment of divine provision. Anchor: Neh 9:25. | 1 |
| `413-002` | H7600 *sha.a.nan* | Term names the divinely-given condition of secure quietness — the ease of God's people living in genuine peace and safety as the fruit of shalom, in contrast to the self-generated complacency of 413-001. The same inner phenomenology (settled, untroubled, at ease) has an entirely different ground: when God gives it, it is the condition of the redeemed community at rest. Anchor: Isa 32:18. | 1 |
| `7109-001` | H2630 *cha.san* | Term (in negation) names the inner disposition of not-hoarding — the eschatological ordering in which material wealth is released for others rather than retained for the self. Isa 23:18: Tyre's merchandise will not be stored or hoarded but will supply those who dwell before the Lord. The negated form names the disordered instinct (hoarding) by pointing to its reversal; the positive content is the ordered act of releasing wealth toward God's people. Anchor: Isa 23:18. | 1 |
| `7582-001` | G4433 *ptōcheuō* | Term names the voluntary poverty of Christ as the paradigmatic act of the wealth domain's inversion — though he was rich he became poor, so that through his poverty we might become rich (2Cor 8:9). This is the foundational Christological statement of M46-D: divine wealth voluntarily surrendered; voluntary impoverishment as the means of others' enrichment; the through-put of self-impoverishment for others' benefit. Every act of voluntary release in M46 participates in or reflects this movement. Anchor: 2Cor 8:9. | 1 |

## §3. Per-term comprehensive detail

Each term gets numbered sections: identity • meaning • anchor-verse linkages • groups • findings • pointers • auxiliary data. Verses are not repeated here — they are listed by sub-group in §2. Items linked only by registry are summarised here; exhaustive lists are in the appendix §4.

<a id="t-H7230"></a>
### H7230 rov — abundance (`mti_id=1142`)

**Identity & meaning**

1. Strong's: `H7230` · Lang: Hebrew · Owner: OWNER
2. Registry: R152 strife · Legacy C-cluster: C18
3. Sub-group: `M46-A` (Inner closure)
4. Term status: extracted · Evidential: — · Occurrences: 152
5. Patch-authoring refs: `mti_id=1142` · `md_version=3` · `vc_status=vc_completed`

**Verses:** 41 distinct locations (see §2 for the verse table grouped by sub-group).

**Group memberships (1)**

| Group ID | Code | Description | Notes |
|---|---|---|---|
| 2213 | 1142-001 | Term names abundance or greatness as a scalar applied to inner-moral realities — the weight or magnitude of iniquity (Jer 13:22; 30:14-15; Lam 1:5), the abundance of steadfast love that answers it (Lam 3:32; Isa 63:7; Neh 13:22), the greatness of spiritual failure (Hos 9:7), and the magnitude of false spiritual resources that cannot save (Isa 47:9-13). Rov does not name wealth in itself but names the weight of what accumulates in the inner-moral domain — and how the magnitude of divine faithfulness exceeds the magnitude of human failure. Anchor: Isa 63:7 (abundance of his steadfast love); Hos 9:7 (great iniquity and great hatred). |  |

**Findings — direct (Strong's mention) (0)**

(none)

**Findings — via anchor verse (2 unique)**

| Finding ID | Type | Matched anchor refs | Excerpt |
|---|---|---|---|
| OBS-062-T2-251 | OBSERVATION | Isa 47:9 | The occult-charming groups (H2267 — spell/charmer; Psa 58:5, Isa 47:9) involve the use of fellowship vocabulary in relation to non-human spiritual forces — charms are a form of joining with occult power. This is the nearest evidence of fellowship operating in relation to spiritual beings. OBS-062-024, OBS-062-035. |
| OBS-060-T2-064 | OBSERVATION | Neh 9:25 | The characteristic deepens memory in its covenantal mode: the accumulated record of divine giving across generations forms the memory-structure of Israel's inner-being identity. Each new giving adds to the memory of prior giving, deepening the narrative foundation of trust. Neh 9 (the long Levitical prayer) is itself a memory-act: reciting the history of divine giving as the basis of present covenantal renewal. Hos 2:15 invokes Exodus memory as the ground of eschatological hope — the memory of what God gave before shapes the expectation of what he will give again. The characteristic does not impair memory in any evidenced instance; it is constitutively tied to the faithfulness of remembered giving. |

**SD pointers — direct (1)**

| Pointer | Priority | Strong's ref | Description |
|---|---|---|---|
| DIM-152-SD001 | MEDIUM | H7230 | rov (H7230, abundance) in the strife registry (group 1142-001) is assigned Divine-Human Correspondence because both anchors have GOD as subject (divine weariness of ritual; divine steadfast love) while related verses carry the human moral-failure pole. This raises a programme-level question: does the biblical vocabulary of magnitude (rov, rabah, and cognates) consistently operate in divine-human correspondence mode — the greatness of divine attributes set against and corresponding to the weight of human moral condition? Session D should examine this across all registries where magnitude vocabulary appears. |

**Auxiliary data**

_Related words (14):_

### H6239 o.sher — riches (`mti_id=7586`)

**Identity & meaning**

1. Strong's: `H6239` · Lang: Hebrew · Owner: —
2. Registry: R215 wealth · Legacy C-cluster: unassigned
3. Sub-group: `M46-A` (Inner closure) · `M46-B` (Hardness and insatiability) · `M46-C` (The ordering of character and wealth)
4. Term status: extracted · Evidential: — · Occurrences: 37
5. Patch-authoring refs: `mti_id=7586` · `md_version=3` · `vc_status=vc_completed`

**Meaning text**

wealth, riches

**Verses:** 36 distinct locations (see §2 for the verse table grouped by sub-group).

**Group memberships (5)**

| Group ID | Code | Description | Notes |
|---|---|---|---|
| 2933 | 7010-001 | Term names material wealth in its relation to the inner life of the person who holds it — its capacity to function as an imagined stronghold (Pro 18:11 — high wall in his imagination), to distort relationship (Pro 19:4 — wealth brings many friends; poor man deserted), to produce insatiability (Pro 30:15 — never says enough), and to reveal the inner person's character in how it is gained and used. Hon in its wisdom-ordered forms (Pro 3:9 — honoring God with wealth; Pro 8:18 — enduring wealth with righteousness; Song 8:7 — cannot purchase love) routes to M46-C. This VCG holds the verses where hon names wealth as the inner-being pressure point. Anchor: Pro 18:11 (imagined stronghold); Pro 30:15 (insatiability). |  |
| 3703 | 7577-001 | Term names the inner-closure condition of the self-assessed wealthy person — the one who says 'I am rich, I need nothing' and thereby closes themselves to receiving from God. Wealth has become both the self-assessment and the perceived need-eliminator. The Laodicean church (Rev 3:17) is the paradigm: materially self-sufficient, spiritually blind and wretched without knowing it. Luk 6:24 names the same pattern from another angle: the rich have received their consolation — their inner satisfaction is fully met in this age, leaving nothing for the age to come. Mat 19:23-24 names the inner obstacle wealth creates for kingdom entry. Anchor: Rev 3:17. | WA-M46-patch-vcg-from-reading-v1-20260514: subgroup=M46-A — New VCG. Primary M46-A inner-closure face of plousios. |
| 3718 | 7584-001 | Term names the inner condition of the wealthy person whose material abundance has produced specific distortions — wise in their own eyes (Pro 28:11); unable to sleep from the restlessness of abundance (Ecc 5:12); answering roughly those who petition (Pro 18:23); exercising structural dominion over the poor (Pro 22:7); full of violence (Mic 6:12); unwilling to give from their own abundance, taking instead from the poor (2Sa 12:4); ignoring the suffering neighbor while feasting daily (Luk 16:19-21); going to bed rich and waking to find wealth gone (Job 27:19). Wealth produces a specific inner posture toward others and toward the person's own condition. Anchor: Pro 28:11. | WA-M46-patch-vcg-from-reading-v1-20260514: subgroup=M46-B — New VCG. M46-B. |
| 3716 | 7586-001 | Term names material riches as the object of misplaced trust and the condition of insatiability — those who trust in their wealth and boast of the abundance of their riches (Psa 49:6); the man who trusted in the abundance of his riches rather than making God his refuge (Psa 52:7); whoever trusts in riches will fall (Pro 11:28); the eyes never satisfied with riches (Ecc 4:8); wealth present but the power to enjoy it absent (Ecc 6:2); riches kept to the owner's hurt and then lost (Ecc 5:13-14); Haman recounting the splendour of his riches to his household (Est 5:11). Material wealth presented as the inner security foundation and found to be unreliable, insatiable, and ultimately alienating. Anchor: Psa 52:7. | WA-M46-patch-vcg-from-reading-v1-20260514: subgroup=M46-B — New VCG. M46-B. |
| 3717 | 7586-002 | Term names material riches as the gift and fruit of the ordered inner life — Wisdom carries riches in her left hand alongside long life in her right (Pro 3:16); riches and honor are with Wisdom, enduring wealth and righteousness (Pro 8:18); the reward of humility and fear of the Lord is riches and honor and life (Pro 22:4); wealth and riches are in the house of the righteous, paired with enduring righteousness (Psa 112:3). Solomon chose wisdom rather than riches and received riches as the unasked-for consequence (1Ki 3:11-13; 2Ch 1:11-12). Both riches and honor come from God who governs all (1Ch 29:12). Anchor: Pro 8:18. | WA-M46-patch-vcg-from-reading-v1-20260514: subgroup=M46-C — New VCG. M46-C. |

<a id="t-G4434"></a>
### G4434 ptōchos — poor (`mti_id=7583`)

**Identity & meaning**

1. Strong's: `G4434` · Lang: Greek · Owner: —
2. Registry: R215 wealth · Legacy C-cluster: unassigned
3. Sub-group: `M46-A` (Inner closure) · `M46-B` (Hardness and insatiability) · `M46-D` (The inner life beyond material circumstance)
4. Term status: extracted · Evidential: — · Occurrences: 122
5. Patch-authoring refs: `mti_id=7583` · `md_version=3` · `vc_status=vc_completed`
6. Mounce short-def: poor; (n.) poor, beggar, a person of few resources, culturally considered oppressed, despised, and miserable. "The poor in spirit" are not lacking in spirit, but have the positive moral quality of humility, realizing they have nothing to offer God but are in need of his free gifts

**Meaning text**

poor; (n.) poor, beggar, a person of few resources, culturally considered oppressed, despised, and miserable. "The poor in spirit" are not lacking in spirit, but have the positive moral quality of humility, realizing they have nothing to offer God but are in need of his free gifts

reduced to beggary, mendicant; poor, indigent, Mt. 19:21; 26:9, 11; metaphorically spiritually poor, Rev. 3:17; by implication a person of low condition, Mt. 11:4; Lk. 4:18; 7:22; metaphorically beggarly, sorry, Gal. 4:9; metaphorically lowly, Mt. 5:3; Lk. 6:20

**Verses:** 34 distinct locations (see §2 for the verse table grouped by sub-group).

**Group memberships (5)**

| Group ID | Code | Description | Notes |
|---|---|---|---|
| 3703 | 7577-001 | Term names the inner-closure condition of the self-assessed wealthy person — the one who says 'I am rich, I need nothing' and thereby closes themselves to receiving from God. Wealth has become both the self-assessment and the perceived need-eliminator. The Laodicean church (Rev 3:17) is the paradigm: materially self-sufficient, spiritually blind and wretched without knowing it. Luk 6:24 names the same pattern from another angle: the rich have received their consolation — their inner satisfaction is fully met in this age, leaving nothing for the age to come. Mat 19:23-24 names the inner obstacle wealth creates for kingdom entry. Anchor: Rev 3:17. | WA-M46-patch-vcg-from-reading-v1-20260514: subgroup=M46-A — New VCG. Primary M46-A inner-closure face of plousios. |
| 3713 | 7580-001 | Term names the enrichment of others as the inner-being goal and outcome of apostolic poverty — as poor yet making many rich (2Cor 6:10); enriched in every way to be generous in every way (2Cor 9:11). The through-put pattern: material poverty is the condition from which relational and spiritual enrichment of others flows. The person who is enriched (ploutizō) by God is enriched not as the terminal destination but as the instrument of further generosity. Anchor: 2Cor 6:10. | WA-M46-patch-vcg-from-reading-v1-20260514: subgroup=M46-D — New VCG. M46-D. |
| 3706 | 7583-001 | Term names poverty as the inner posture of dependence and openness before God — the condition in which the person has nothing to offer and is therefore constitutively receptive to divine gift. The poor in spirit (Mat 5:3) are not people lacking in spirit but those who know they have no inner resource to bring; the kingdom belongs to them. The materially poor are the primary recipients of the gospel (Luk 4:18; 4:20) and the heirs of the kingdom (Luk 6:20; Jam 2:5). God has specifically chosen the ptōchos of this world to be rich in faith. Material poverty may be the condition that most naturally removes the inner buffer of self-sufficiency and creates openness. Anchor: Mat 5:3. | WA-M46-patch-vcg-from-reading-v1-20260514: subgroup=M46-D — New VCG. Primary M46-D poverty-as-kingdom-ground face. |
| 3707 | 7583-002 | Term names the poor person as the moral test of the wealthy person's inner condition — the assembly that pays attention to the rich visitor and tells the poor man to stand reveals what is in the heart (Jam 2:2-3); the community that dishonors the poor while honoring the rich has made a moral judgment about persons based on material condition; the false concern of Judas for the poor (Joh 12:6) reveals that invoking the poor can itself be an inner act of concealment. How a person treats the ptōchos is the index of their inner moral condition. Anchor: Jam 2:2-3. | WA-M46-patch-vcg-from-reading-v1-20260514: subgroup=M46-B — New VCG. M46-B — poor as the mirror of the wealthy person's inner condition. |
| 3708 | 7583-003 | Term names voluntary giving from poverty and the command to give to the poor as acts that produce genuine enrichment and kingdom treasure. The widow who gave all her living gave more than all the wealthy (Luk 21:3; Mar 12:43) — her gift was total and was evaluated by Jesus as greater. The commands to give to the poor (Luk 18:22; Mat 19:21) are the specific acts of release by which kingdom treasure is obtained. Zacchaeus giving half to the poor is the spontaneous inner-being response to encounter with Jesus (Luk 19:8). The giving to the poor is both the external act and the inner act of relinquishing the attachment that wealth creates. Anchor: Luk 21:3. | WA-M46-patch-vcg-from-reading-v1-20260514: subgroup=M46-D — New VCG. M46-D — giving to the poor as act of release producing kingdom treasure. |

rich, opulent, wealthy;, and pl. οἱ πλούσιοι, the rich, Mat.19:23, 24; 27:57; metaphorically rich, abounding in, distinguished for, Eph. 2:4; Jas. 2:5; Rev. 2:9; 3:17; rich in glory, dignity, bliss, etc., 2Cor. 8:9

The plousios vocabulary is here deployed in its reversed form: the true wealth is not material, and the materially poor may be the genuinely rich. Cho.sen also names the true treasure: the fear of the Lord is Zion's cho.sen — the inner realities of salvation, wisdom, and knowledge are the real wealth, which material cho.sen only shadows. Wealth obtained through violence (Eze 22:25) and wealth that does not last (Pro 27:24) complete the picture. | 3716 | 7586-001 | Term names material riches as the object of misplaced trust and the condition of insatiability — those who trust in their wealth and boast of the abundance of their riches (Psa 49:6); the man who trusted in the abundance of his riches rather than making God his refuge (Psa 52:7); whoever trusts in riches will fall (Pro 11:28); the eyes never satisfied with riches (Ecc 4:8); wealth present but the power to enjoy it absent (Ecc 6:2); riches kept to the owner's hurt and then lost (Ecc 5:13-14); Haman recounting the splendour of his riches to his household (Est 5:11). Material wealth presented as the inner security foundation and found to be unreliable, insatiable, and ultimately alienating. Solomon chose wisdom rather than riches and received riches as the unasked-for consequence (1Ki 3:11-13; 2Ch 1:11-12). Both riches and honor come from God who governs all (1Ch 29:12). **Identity & meaning**

1. Strong's: `H6223` · Lang: Hebrew · Owner: —
2. Registry: R215 wealth · Legacy C-cluster: unassigned

5. Patch-authoring refs: `mti_id=7584` · `md_version=3` · `vc_status=vc_completed`

**Meaning text**

adj

1) rich, wealthy

n

2) the rich, the wealthy, rich man

**Verses:** 23 distinct locations (see §2 for the verse table grouped by sub-group).

|---|---|---|---|
| 2933 | 7010-001 | Term names material wealth in its relation to the inner life of the person who holds it — its capacity to function as an imagined stronghold (Pro 18:11 — high wall in his imagination), to distort relationship (Pro 19:4 — wealth brings many friends; poor man deserted), to produce insatiability (Pro 30:15 — never says enough), and to reveal the inner person's character in how it is gained and used. | 3703 | 7577-001 | Term names the inner-closure condition of the self-assessed wealthy person — the one who says 'I am rich, I need nothing' and thereby closes themselves to receiving from God. The Laodicean church (Rev 3:17) is the paradigm: materially self-sufficient, spiritually blind and wretched without knowing it. | 3718 | 7584-001 | Term names the inner condition of the wealthy person whose material abundance has produced specific distortions — wise in their own eyes (Pro 28:11); unable to sleep from the restlessness of abundance (Ecc 5:12); answering roughly those who petition (Pro 18:23); exercising structural dominion over the poor (Pro 22:7); full of violence (Mic 6:12); unwilling to give from their own abundance, taking instead from the poor (2Sa 12:4); ignoring the suffering neighbor while feasting daily (Luk 16:19-21); going to bed rich and waking to find wealth gone (Job 27:19). Wealth produces a specific inner posture toward others and toward the person's own condition. Anchor: Pro 28:11. | WA-M46-patch-vcg-from-reading-v1-20260514: subgroup=M46-B — New VCG. M46-B. |
| 3719 | 7584-002 | Term names the rich person in the wisdom tradition's moral examination — the rich and poor stand equally before God who made them both (Pro 22:2); better is the poor person who walks in integrity than the rich person who is crooked in their ways (Pro 28:6); the rich man unwilling to give from his own flock reveals a specific inner moral failure (2Sa 12:4 — moral lesson context). The wisdom tradition consistently evaluates the a.shir not by their material condition but by their inner character — and regularly finds that integrity in poverty exceeds wealth in crookedness. Anchor: Pro 22:2. | WA-M46-patch-vcg-from-reading-v1-20260514: subgroup=M46-C — New VCG. M46-C. |

| OBS-103-T2-043 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Rut 3:10 | Yes — emphatically. The commanded form of love ('you shall love' — Mat 22:37, Lev 19:18) requires the will's exercise — love cannot be merely a spontaneous feeling if it is commanded. Enemy-love (Mat 5:44, OBS-103-143) is the clearest evidence: loving enemies requires volitional engagement against natural preference. The ranking of loves (Mat 10:37, OBS-103-151) requires a deliberate choice-act — placing love of Christ above natural love of family. Ruth's chesed (Rut 3:10, OBS-103-089) — choosing the elderly Boaz over young men — is love expressed through will against self-interest. The will's engagement is not peripheral but central to love in its highest form (agapē, chesed). The affective forms (phileō, dod) may be less volitionally constituted, but the command-forms require the will. |
| OBS-103-T2-044 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Rut 3:10 | Both operate in the data — love both requires and produces will-acts. It requires the will: enemy-love (Mat 5:44), ranking of loves (Mat 10:37), and Ruth's choice (Rut 3:10) are all volitional love-acts. It produces will-acts: love of Christ produces commandment-keeping (Joh 14:15, OBS-103-140) — the inner orientation generates the volitional pattern of life. The two modes are distinct: love-as-act-of-will (requiring deliberate engagement) vs love-as-generator-of-will (the inner orientation that produces volitional patterns). Both are evidenced. The affective forms (phileō, dod) may operate without explicit volitional initiation, but they too produce will-acts (Peter's fileō generates faithful mission-response, Joh 21:17, OBS-103-153). |
| OBS-103-T2-075 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Rut 3:10 | Love as given outward is the primary ethical direction of the commandment structure: love your neighbour (Lev 19:18), love your enemy (Mat 5:44), love one another (Joh 13:34). The giving of love reaches its maximum in Joh 15:13 (OBS-103-125) — 'greater love has no one than this: that someone lay down his life/soul for his friends.' The gift of the soul is love's outer limit. The giving-forms span: chesed as daily covenantal faithfulness given to the covenant-partner (OBS-103-089); agapē given to the enemy against natural preference (OBS-103-143); phileō given as warmth within the community (OBS-103-119); rachamim given as compassion toward the suffering (OBS-103-105). Love-as-given is the primary ethical form in which the inner orientation is concretely actualised. |
| OBS-103-T2-083 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Rut 3:10 | Yes — explicitly. 1Th 3:12 (OBS-103-130): 'may the Lord make you increase and abound in love' — love admits of increase; the Lord is the agent of its growth. The prayer-form implies that love's increase is both desirable and possible, dependent on the Lord's action. The degree-language in the data also implies growth: Rut 3:10 — 'you have made this last kindness greater than the first' — chesed measurably greater in a later act than an earlier one. Love is not fixed at a static level but admits of deepening expression and increase over time. |

| OBS-103-T2-154 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Rut 3:10 | Love **enables and extends** agency beyond its natural range. The key evidence: enemy-love (Mat 5:44) produces agency toward those the person would naturally avoid — love extends the agentive reach to include hostile others. Joh 15:13 (OBS-103-125): love produces the maximum agentive act (laying down the soul) that no other motivation could reliably sustain. Ruth's chesed-love (Rut 3:10, OBS-103-089) produces the agentive choice against self-interest — love expands agency by overriding the self-protective constraints that would otherwise limit it. Disordered love produces distorted agency: love-of-money produces the agency of departure from faith (1Ti 6:10); love-of-primacy produces the agency of exclusion (3Jo 9, OBS-103-155). The direction of love determines the direction and quality of agency — love is the inner source from which the person acts outward. |
| OBS-099-T2-006 | OBSERVATION | Rut 3:10 | Kindness instantiates the outward-oriented relational disposition of God toward others. <a id="t-G4149"></a>
### G4149 ploutos — riches (`mti_id=7581`)

**Identity & meaning**

1. Strong's: `G4149` · Lang: Greek · Owner: —
2. Registry: R215 wealth · Legacy C-cluster: unassigned
3. Sub-group: `M46-A` (Inner closure) · `M46-B` (Hardness and insatiability) · `M46-D` (The inner life beyond material circumstance)
4. Term status: extracted · Evidential: — · Occurrences: 98
5. Patch-authoring refs: `mti_id=7581` · `md_version=3` · `vc_status=vc_completed`
6. Mounce short-def: riches, wealth

**Meaning text**

riches, wealth, opulence, Mt. 13:22; Lk. 8:14; in NT, πλοῦτος τοῦ Θεοῦ, or Χριστοῦ, those rich benefits, those abundant blessings which flow from God or Christ, Eph. 3:8; Phil. 4:19; meton. richness, abundance, Rom. 2:4; 11:33; 2Cor. 8:2; metonymy a spiritual enriching, Rom. 11:12

**Verses:** 21 distinct locations (see §2 for the verse table grouped by sub-group).

**Group memberships (3)**

| Group ID | Code | Description | Notes |
|---|---|---|---|
| 3703 | 7577-001 | Term names the inner-closure condition of the self-assessed wealthy person — the one who says 'I am rich, I need nothing' and thereby closes themselves to receiving from God. Wealth has become both the self-assessment and the perceived need-eliminator. The Laodicean church (Rev 3:17) is the paradigm: materially self-sufficient, spiritually blind and wretched without knowing it. Luk 6:24 names the same pattern from another angle: the rich have received their consolation — their inner satisfaction is fully met in this age, leaving nothing for the age to come. Mat 19:23-24 names the inner obstacle wealth creates for kingdom entry. Anchor: Rev 3:17. | WA-M46-patch-vcg-from-reading-v1-20260514: subgroup=M46-A — New VCG. Primary M46-A inner-closure face of plousios. |
| 3709 | 7581-001 | Term names material riches as the choking and deceiving inner force — the deceitfulness (apatē) of riches competes with the word of God for the inner person's receptivity and produces fruitlessness (Mat 13:22; Mar 4:19; Luk 8:14); riches that have been unjustly accumulated rot and are moth-eaten (Jam 5:2); the person who has riches set their hope on something inherently uncertain (1Ti 6:17). The word apatē names the mechanism: riches present themselves as satisfying and securing but they are deceptive — they do not deliver what they promise, they choke rather than nourish, they decay rather than endure. Anchor: Mat 13:22. | WA-M46-patch-vcg-from-reading-v1-20260514: subgroup=M46-B — New VCG. M46-B insatiability/deceitfulness face. |
| 3710 | 7581-002 | Term names riches in their revaluation by a higher inner-being calculus — the wealth of generosity that flows from poverty and overflowing joy (2Cor 8:2); the reproach of Christ counted as greater wealth than the treasures of Egypt because Moses was looking to the reward (Heb 11:26). In both cases the person's inner vision — what they are looking toward, what they count as truly valuable — has reordered the wealth calculus. The ploutos of genuine generosity exceeds material ploutos; the ploutos of Christ's reproach exceeds the treasures of Egypt. Anchor: Heb 11:26. | WA-M46-patch-vcg-from-reading-v1-20260514: subgroup=M46-D — New VCG. M46-D revaluation face. |

**Findings — direct (Strong's mention) (0)**

**Findings — via anchor verse (47 unique)**

| Finding ID | Type | Matched anchor refs | Excerpt |
|---|---|---|---|
| DIM-42-003 | DIMENSION_REVIEW | Luk 8:14 | Group 3090-001 (hēdonē — pleasure as the competing inner orientation, desires that war within) occupies a significant counter-position to the positive delight vocabulary. Pleasure as the adversary of the will toward God is a distinctive NT inner-being concept. Session B should examine the hēdonē / delight tension as a structural feature of the inner life. |
| OBS-067-T2-002 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Eph 2:7 | Scripture attributes goodness to God extensively and explicitly. Psa 119:68 ("You are good"), Psa 34:8 ("the Lord is good"), Psa 100:5 and Ps 106:1 / 107:1 / 118:1 (doxological refrain "for he is good"), Lam 3:25 ("the Lord is good to those who wait for him"), Rom 11:22 (God's chrēstotēs), Eph 2:7 (God's chrēstotēs in Christ). The attribution reveals that goodness in the human person is not an independent achievement but a participation in and reflection of an attribute that originates in God. Human goodness is derivative by design — it is what being made in the image of the good God looks like in a creature. This has direct significance: the human person who embodies goodness is expressing their created nature; the human person who fails to do so is in a condition of departure from original design. (OBS-067-OBS-001, OBS-067-OBS-006) |
| OBS-067-T2-005 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Eph 2:7 | The evidence is suggestive but not fully explicit. 2Th 1:11 ("may God fulfil every resolve for good and every work of faith by his power") implies an eschatological completeness — the resolve for good is present now, but its fulfilment is anticipated as God's completing act. Eph 2:7 places God's display of chrēstotēs "in the coming ages" — goodness has a future-facing dimension of fullness. Gal 5:22 as Spirit-fruit connects goodness to the present work of the Spirit that anticipates the fullness of Spirit-transformation. However, the data does not contain a verse that explicitly narrates goodness as moving toward eschatological fullness in the way that glory or resurrection does. The eschatological trajectory is implied by the pneumatological framework (Spirit-production → Spirit-completion) but not explicitly named. This is a partial gap. (OBS-067-OBS-008, OBS-067-OBS-016) |
| OBS-067-T2-009 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Eph 2:7, Rom 2:4 | The covenantal typological dimension is present and clear: God's good word (884-005, Jos 23:14) and his chrēstotēs displayed supremely in Christ (Eph 2:7) both carry a Christological register — the fullness of divine goodness is displayed in Christ's person and work. Rom 2:4 names chrēstotēs as the mechanism through which God leads to repentance, which is structurally a Christological movement. However, the data does not contain an explicit typological argument (e.g., the good king who typifies the messianic king, or a creation-goodness → new-creation-goodness typology). The christological and eschatological dimensions are present in NT usage but not through formal typological structure. This is a partial finding — the direction is named but the full typological structure is not explicitly drawn in the verse evidence. (OBS-067-OBS-006, OBS-067-OBS-036) |
| OBS-067-T2-024 | VERSE_PATTERN | Rom 2:4 | The immediate response varies by context and subject. When the human encounters divine goodness: trust, praise, and receptive desire (as above). When the person encounters not-goodness (their own — Eze 36:31): the immediate response is self-loathing and moral awareness — the recognition of deeds as not-good immediately produces self-directed revulsion. When the person encounters divine chrēstotēs (Rom 2:4): the immediate inner movement is toward repentance — the goodness of God produces a first response of conviction and turning. The variation is structured: different registers of goodness encounter produce different first responses, but each response is coherent with the register. Divine goodness in its fullness produces trust; divine goodness as conviction produces repentance; human not-goodness recognised produces loathing. (OBS-067-OBS-035, OBS-067-OBS-025, SBF-067-Q031) |
| OBS-067-T2-074 | VERSE_PATTERN | Rom 2:4 | Conscience is engaged most directly at Eze 36:31: "you will remember your evil ways, and your deeds that were not good, and you will loathe yourselves for your iniquities and your abominations." The self-loathing described is a conscience response — the inner witness of moral failure producing conviction. The sequence (remember → recognise as not-good → loathe) is a conscience sequence: the moral inner witness activates, names the failure, and produces the appropriate inner response (loathing/conviction). Rom 2:4's chrēstotēs "leading to repentance" also implies a conscience engagement — God's kindness acts on the inner being's conscience to produce the movement toward repentance. The goodness-conscience connection operates through the negative register: the recognition of not-goodness activates conscience; the encounter with divine goodness produces the conditions in which conscience can operate truthfully. (OBS-067-OBS-035, OBS-067-OBS-049, SBF-067-Q049) |
| OBS-067-T2-075 | VERSE_PATTERN | Rom 2:4 | Goodness enables and deepens conscience — specifically through the not-goodness register. The recognition of not-goodness (884-006 and Eze 36:31) is a conscience operation enabled by the standard of the good: only a person who has access to the standard of genuine goodness can accurately recognise their own not-goodness and respond with appropriate conviction. The distortion form (Mal 2:17 / Jer 44:17) shows conscience impaired: when goodness is redefined, the conscience's witness becomes unreliable — it applies the wrong standard and produces false moral comfort. Divine chrēstotēs (Rom 2:4) deepens conscience by making repentance both possible and compelling — God's goodness creates the conditions in which the conscience can function toward its proper end (turning). (OBS-067-OBS-021, OBS-067-OBS-035, OBS-067-OBS-043) |
| OBS-067-T2-076 | VERSE_PATTERN | Rom 2:4 | The conscience engagement reveals that goodness functions as the calibrating standard for the conscience's operation. Conscience requires a standard against which it measures; goodness provides that standard. This means that the health of the conscience is tied to the health of the goodness-characteristic: a corrupted goodness standard produces a corrupted conscience witness; a genuine goodness standard enables a truthful conscience. The Eze 36:31 sequence — divine action producing moral self-recognition and loathing — suggests that the restoration of truthful conscience requires a prior restoration of the goodness standard. Goodness and conscience are structurally co-dependent: conscience witnesses accurately to the degree that the good it witnesses against is genuine. (OBS-067-OBS-021, OBS-067-OBS-035, SBF-067-Q049) |
| OBS-067-T2-084 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Rom 2:4 | The evidence reveals a structural tension between the unconditional and conditional registers — and this tension is itself the analytical finding. On the unconditional side: Gen 1:31 establishes goodness as a creative-ontological act prior to any human response — God declares creation good without condition. The doxological refrain "for he is good, his steadfast love endures forever" names an enduring, non-contingent character attribute. On the conditional side: Rom 11:22 names a condition on the ongoing experience of divine chrēstotēs — "provided you continue in his kindness. Otherwise you too will be cut off." This is not a condition on God's character but on the human's ongoing reception of its benefits. Rom 2:4 implies responsiveness — God's kindness "is meant to lead you to repentance," with an implied expectation that repentance follows. The resolution: God's goodness as a character attribute is unconditional; the human's ongoing experience of its relational benefits is covenantal-responsive. This distinction is analytically significant and carries to SP-067-018 (the conditionality question). (OBS-067-OBS-006, OBS-067-OBS-043, OBS-067-OBS-044, SP-067-018) |
| OBS-067-T2-085 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Eph 2:7 | God's extension of goodness reveals a disposition of initiatory, generous, and sustaining goodwill toward the human person. Three dimensions: (1) Initiatory — God acts first in goodness (Gen 1:31; Eph 2:7 — "so that in the coming ages he might show the immeasurable riches of his grace in kindness toward us in Christ Jesus"). The initiative is always divine. (2) Generous — chrēstotēs names not merely adequate goodness but generous goodwill (886-001): God's disposition is one of overflowing benevolence. (3) Sustaining — the covenantal promise (884-005, Jos 23:14: "not one word has failed") names a goodness that holds through time and maintains its commitment. The disposition is not reactive but proactive, generous, and reliable. Lam 3:25 ("the Lord is good to those who wait for him") names waiting as the human posture corresponding to this divine sustaining goodness — the relationship is not passive but involves a human receptive posture. (OBS-067-OBS-006, OBS-067-OBS-033, OBS-067-OBS-034, SBF-067-Q006) |
| OBS-067-T2-096 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Rom 2:4 | The evidence supports both covenantal and cross-covenantal operation. Within covenantal contexts: 884-005 (God's covenantal word, Jos 23:14), 886-001 and 886-002 (Spirit-produced goodness in the new covenant community). These are explicitly covenantal. Cross-covenantal: H2896A in 884-002's moral character register applies to human moral life broadly — the prophetic verdict on not-good conduct (884-006) is addressed to covenant people but the ethical standard (Mic 6:8) has a universal logic. The creation-goodness of Gen 1:31 (884-007) is pre-covenantal — God's goodness extends to all creation before any covenant is formed. Rom 2:4 ("God's kindness leads you to repentance") is addressed to those outside the covenant community as well as within — the mechanism of divine chrēstotēs is not restricted to the covenant. (OBS-067-OBS-006, OBS-067-OBS-036, OBS-067-OBS-043) |
| OBS-067-T2-097 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Rom 2:4 | The relational scope of goodness is wide but not fully mapped in the data. Inclusion evidenced: creation as a whole (Gen 1:31 — all that God made is good); covenant community (Jos 23:14; Col 3:12; Rom 15:14); those outside the covenant who receive divine chrēstotēs leading to repentance (Rom 2:4); the soul who seeks (Lam 3:25 — the seeking person, without boundary). Possible exclusion: Rom 11:22 names "those who have fallen" as the recipients of severity rather than chrēstotēs — but this is presented as a relational condition rather than a categorical exclusion. The data does not name any class of persons categorically excluded from the scope of divine goodness; the blocking conditions (Q&A-032) describe inner orientations that close reception, not divine decisions to exclude. Whether the human expression of goodness has specific scope limits is not directly addressed. Partial gap. (OBS-067-OBS-006, OBS-067-OBS-043, OBS-067-OBS-044) |
| OBS-067-T2-101 | VERSE_PATTERN | Rom 2:4 | Two distinct sequences are named. Sequence 1 — positive transformation arc (divine goodness → reception → reorientation): (a) Encounter with divine goodness — Psa 34:8 ("taste and see"); (b) Trust and refuge — "blessed is the man who takes refuge in him"; (c) Stable inner orientation — Psa 73:28 ("it is good to be near God; I have made the Lord God my refuge"); (d) Overflow toward others — "that I may tell of all your works" (Psa 73:28 conclusion). Sequence 2 — moral awareness arc (recognition → repentance → transformation): (a) Memory of deeds — Eze 36:31 ("you will remember your evil ways"); (b) Recognition as not-good — "and your deeds that were not good"; (c) Self-loathing — "you will loathe yourselves"; (d) Movement toward repentance — implied by context (Eze 36:26 God's Spirit given follows 36:31's loathing). Rom 2:4 names the divine chrēstotēs → repentance sequence directly. Both sequences are coherent and traceable in the verse evidence. (OBS-067-OBS-025, OBS-067-OBS-035, OBS-067-OBS-029, SBF-067-Q031) |
| OBS-067-T2-103 | VERSE_PATTERN | Rom 2:4 | Three distinct mechanisms are evidenced, corresponding to the three transformation types identified at Q&A-123: (1) Encounter mechanism — Psa 34:8 / Psa 73:28: the mechanism is perceptual encounter with divine goodness. The change is produced by tasting and seeing — a direct experiential contact that reorients the inner being. This is neither discipline nor gradual formation but encounter-transformation. (2) Spirit-infusion mechanism — Gal 5:22 / Rom 15:14: the Spirit fills the person with goodness. The mechanism is pneumatological infusion — goodness as fruit is the Spirit's work in the inner being. This is not achieved by effort but received as Spirit-gift. (3) Recognition-confrontation mechanism — Eze 36:31 / Rom 2:4: the mechanism is confrontation with one's own not-goodness in the presence of the divine goodness standard. Memory + recognition + loathing = the mechanism of moral transformation toward goodness. All three mechanisms are present; they are not competing but operate in different registers and at different moments. (OBS-067-OBS-025, OBS-067-OBS-035, OBS-067-OBS-039, SBF-067-Q031) |
| OBS-067-T2-109 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Eph 2:7 | Partial eschatological evidence is present. Eph 2:7 ("so that in the coming ages he might show the immeasurable riches of his grace in kindness toward us in Christ Jesus") explicitly places chrēstotēs in a future-oriented eschatological frame — God's chrēstotēs will be displayed in its fullness in the coming ages. This names an eschatological trajectory: present experience of divine kindness anticipates its future unrestricted display. 2Th 1:11 ("fulfil every resolve for good") implies a completion — God's completing act brings present goodness-resolve to its fullness. However, the specifically eschatological texture of goodness (what it will be in the new creation, what fullness means) is not directly described in the anchor verses beyond these two instances. The trajectory is indicated but not fully developed. (OBS-067-OBS-008, OBS-067-OBS-016, SBF-067-Q006) |
| OBS-067-T2-110 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Eph 2:7 | Eph 2:7 implies that present experience of chrēstotēs is a foretaste of the "immeasurable riches" of its future expression — present goodness is real but partial relative to its coming fullness. The "immeasurable" qualifier suggests that the eschatological form of goodness exceeds present experience in scale and richness. 2Th 1:11's "fulfil every resolve for good" implies that present resolves for goodness, even when genuine, are not yet complete — their completion is a divine eschatological act. The present experience of goodness therefore anticipates: (1) A fullness of divine kindness-display that surpasses present experience; (2) A completion of human goodness-resolve that requires divine power to finish. The gap is that the data does not describe what the experiential content of eschatological goodness will be — only that it will be fuller and completed. (OBS-067-OBS-008, OBS-067-OBS-016) |
| OBS-067-T2-113 | CROSS_REGISTRY | Rom 2:4 | Two sequential relationships are directly named: (1) Goodness → repentance (Rom 2:4): "God's kindness is meant to lead you to repentance" — chrēstotēs explicitly precedes and produces repentance. The sequence is causal: divine goodness is prior, repentance follows. (2) Goodness → faith/trust (Psa 34:8): encounter with divine goodness ("taste and see that the Lord is good") is followed by "blessed is the man who takes refuge in him" — goodness is the experiential prior that produces the faith/refuge response. Goodness precedes faith in the encounter sequence. A third sequence is implied by SBF-067-Q133 (prior analysis): goodness is prior to faith (encounter with goodness → faith response); 2Th 1:11 shows them as parallel co-products in the other direction. These sequential findings are confirmed and structured. (OBS-067-OBS-025, OBS-067-OBS-043, SBF-067-Q127, SBF-067-Q133) |
| OBS-067-T2-114 | CROSS_REGISTRY | Rom 2:4 | The goodness → repentance sequence (Rom 2:4) is explicitly causal: "is meant to lead to" — divine chrēstotēs is the mechanism producing repentance. The goodness → faith sequence (Psa 34:8) is causal-enabling: the experiential encounter with goodness enables and produces the trust response. The goodness-and-faith parallel sequence (2Th 1:11: "every resolve for good and every work of faith") is correlational — they are co-produced by God's power rather than one causing the other. The three sequential relationships reveal three different structural relationships: causal (goodness produces repentance), enabling-causal (goodness enables faith-response), and correlational (goodness-resolve and faith-work as parallel Spirit-expressions). (OBS-067-OBS-025, OBS-067-OBS-043, SBF-067-Q127) |
| OBS-067-T2-115 | CROSS_REGISTRY | Rom 2:4 | Two producing relationships are directly evidenced: (1) Goodness produces repentance — Rom 2:4: divine chrēstotēs → repentance (mechanism: moral conviction and drawing). (2) Goodness produces trust/faith — Psa 34:8: encounter with divine goodness → trust-response (mechanism: experiential encounter enabling faith). A third is implied: goodness produces praise — the doxological group (884-001) is the response to divine goodness; the encounter produces the praise. These are all divine-to-human sequences: God's goodness produces these characteristics in the human inner being. The human expression of goodness in community (Rom 15:14) produces instruction-capacity in others — goodness produces formation-capacity. (OBS-067-OBS-025, OBS-067-OBS-043, SBF-067-Q004) |
| OBS-067-T2-141 | VERSE_ANNOTATION | Rom 2:4 | Three key argument structures: (1) Mic 6:8 — definitional argument: if goodness is defined as X, and X consists of justice + kindness + humility, then goodness is not a simple quality but a tri-fold orientation. The logical structure is definitional-analytical. (2) Rom 2:4 — teleological argument: God's chrēstotēs has a purpose ("meant to lead you to repentance") — the argument is that the purpose of divine goodness is the human's turning. The logical structure is purposive. (3) Rom 11:22 — conditional argument: God's kindness operates under a conditional that requires human continuance ("provided you continue in his kindness"). The logical structure is conditional. These three argument structures (definitional, purposive, conditional) represent three different logical relationships between goodness and other inner-being realities. (OBS-067-OBS-027, OBS-067-OBS-043, OBS-067-OBS-044, SBF-067-Q042) |
| OBS-064-T2-149 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Eph 1:7 | Yes — the salach root family deploys typologically. The OT cultic atonement-forgiveness (H5545 — "they shall be forgiven") operates typologically toward the NT's atonement through Christ's blood (Eph 1:7 — "redemption through his blood, the forgiveness of our trespasses"). The sacrificial system typologically anticipates the definitive atonement. Isa 53:5 (shared anchor with R030 contrition) extends this: the Servant's wounds produce peace and healing — the typological mechanism is suffering-as-substitution producing release-of-debt. OBS-064-014, OBS-064-028. |
| OBS-064-T2-150 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Eph 1:7 | Typological direction: from cultic-formulaic (OT sacrificial system — external mechanism producing declared forgiveness) to personal-atoning (Christ's blood as the definitive sacrifice — producing actual forgiveness of trespasses, Col 2:13 — "having forgiven us all our trespasses"). The typology moves from mechanism to its fulfilment, from shadow to substance. OBS-064-014, OBS-064-028. |
| OBS-064-T2-189 | SOMATIC_EVIDENCE | Eph 1:7 | The direction runs from somatic compassion inward and upward to the volitional act (Mat 18:27 — somatic response → forgiveness decision). In the atonement mechanism, the direction runs from somatic suffering outward to released debt-status in others (Isa 53:5; Eph 1:7). OBS-064-022, OBS-064-028. |
| OBS-064-T2-226 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Eph 1:7 | Yes — the primary and dominant relational direction in this registry. God is the exclusive subject of H5545 (salach). The NT forgiveness pronouncements (Luk 7:47-48, Mar 2:5) are divine-to-human. Eph 1:7 ("through his blood, the forgiveness of our trespasses") names the divine mechanism. Col 2:13 ("having forgiven us all our trespasses") — divine agentive act. OBS-064-003, OBS-064-022. |

Dan 9:19 ("for your own sake") grounds the intercessory plea entirely in God's own character. Eph 1:7 grounds forgiveness in Christ's blood — the divine provision, not human performance. God's declaration ("your sins are forgiven") or the human's inner release creates the new condition. (2) Atoning mechanism — the blood of Christ (Eph 1:7) is the structural ground that enables the declaration. The atonement mechanism is the basis; the declaration is the application.
