| OBS-117-T2-053 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Num 6:26, Isa 54:10, Joh 14:27, Isa 26:3, Gal 5:22 | The data establishes a primary origin and two secondary origins: (1) Primary — bestowed by God: the overwhelming weight of the evidence shows shalom as divine gift. The Aaronic blessing (Num 6:26 — "the Lord... give you peace"), God's covenant of peace (Isa 54:10), God speaking peace to his saints (Psa 85:8), God planning welfare (Jer 29:11), Christ as the peace-giver (Joh 14:27 — "my peace I give to you"), Spirit-fruit peace (Gal 5:22) — all establish God as the constitutional source. The human person cannot generate authentic shalom from within. (2) Secondary — formed from within through orientation: Isa 26:3 — the mind stayed on God produces perfect peace; the person's own cognitive act of orientation enables the reception of what God provides. This is not generation from within but reception through orientation; (3) Generated falsely from within: the false-shalom pattern (Deu 29:19, Jer 6:14) — the person can generate a counterfeit inner condition of peace by self-deception or false claim, but this is precisely what the data condemns. Authentic constitutional origin: divine gift received through trust and covenant orientation. |
| OBS-117-T2-054 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Num 6:26, Isa 53:5, Gal 5:22, Joh 14:27 | The origin is singular in ground but multiple in mechanism. Ground: God alone is the ultimate source — whether through covenant (2508-002), through Christ (426-001, Eph 2:14), or through the Spirit (Gal 5:22), all peace traces back to God as its constitutional source. Mechanism: three distinct modes of origination appear in the data: (1) Covenantal gift — God gives shalom through covenant (Num 6:26, Isa 54:10); the covenant is the structural channel; (2) Christological accomplishment — peace purchased through the cross (Isa 53:5, Col 1:20, G1517 430-001); Christ's act constitutes the peace; (3) Pneumatological formation — peace formed as Spirit-fruit (Gal 5:22); the Spirit produces peace over time within the person. These three mechanisms correspond to the three persons of the Trinity — the origin is Trinitarian in structure, though the data does not use that language explicitly. The singular ground (God) and multiple mechanisms prevent both monism (peace from only one channel) and pluralism (peace from multiple independent sources). |
| OBS-117-T2-055 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Deu 28:65, Psa 32:3, Gal 5:22, Phili 4:7, Psa 4:8, Isa 53:5 | Yes — and this is the most analytically significant T2 finding for the peace registry. The constitutional movement of peace is extensively documented, operating in multiple directions: (1) Spirit → soul → body (downward cascade under loss of peace): The absence of peace radiates from the inner person outward: Deu 28:65 — peacelessness produces trembling heart (volitional/heart level) + failing eyes (body level) + languishing soul (soul level); Psa 32:3 — suppressed inner condition → bones wasting (body); Psa 38:3 — sin disrupting inner peace → no soundness in flesh, no health in bones. The movement under un-peace: inner disorder → somatic deterioration. (2) God → spirit → soul → body (downward cascade under the gift of peace): Gal 5:22 (Spirit produces peace as fruit) → Phili 4:7 (peace guards hearts and minds) → Psa 4:8 (peace enables the body to lie down and sleep). The gift moves from its divine source through the spirit/Spirit level into the heart and mind, and from there into somatic rest. (3) Body → inner (the redemptive reversal): Isa 53:5 — the Servant's wounded body → peace received in others' inner being; the movement against the normal direction, accomplished through the cross. |
| OBS-117-T2-056 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Psa 32:3, Psa 4:8, Isa 53:5, Phili 4:7 | Three patterns are identifiable: (1) Cascading loss pattern: inner peace → somatic peace is the normal condition; inner dis-peace → somatic deterioration follows the causal chain inner-to-body (Psa 32:3, 38:3, Deu 28:65). The sequence is consistent: first the inner person is disordered (by sin, by judgment, by absent trust), then the body registers the disorder. The body is the last to fail and the indicator of deep inner trouble. (2) Cascading gift pattern: God gives peace (Num 6:26, Joh 14:27) → heart and mind receive it (Phili 4:7, Isa 26:3) → body enacts it (Psa 4:8 — lying down in sleep). The sequence moves from divine source through inner-being faculties into somatic expression. The body's rest is the downstream expression of the inner peace received. (3) Redemptive reversal pattern: suffering body of the Servant (Isa 53:5) → inner peace produced in others; this reversal moves counter to the normal direction and is unique in the corpus. The three patterns together establish peace as a constitutionally mobile characteristic — it does not reside statically in one location but flows through the whole person, and flows in different directions under different conditions. |
| OBS-117-T2-057 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Deu 28:65, 1Ki 19:12, Psa 131:2 | Yes — peace engages perception primarily in its negative form: the perceptive faculty is disturbed, dulled, or altered when peace is absent. Evidence: (1) Deu 28:65 — "failing eyes" as a somatic expression of peacelessness; the eyes (a perceptive organ) fail under the condition of un-peace in judgment; (2) The silence vocabulary in this registry (H1827 demamah, G4602 sigē) names the threshold-quiet before perception of the divine voice — 1Ki 19:12 (still small voice after the demamah-silence), OBS-117-069 from prior session; the silence that is peace's companion is precisely the condition that enables divine perception; (3) Psa 131:2 — "I have calmed and quieted my soul... like a weaned child" — the quieted soul is positioned for receptive perception; restlessness prevents this. |
| OBS-117-T2-058 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | 1Ki 19:12, Psa 131:2, Deu 28:65 | Peace enables perception — specifically, the silence/stillness dimension of peace enables the perception of the divine voice and divine reality. The pattern: demamah (profound silence, H1827) is the threshold before divine speech (1Ki 19:12, Job 4:16 — prior session OBS-117-069, 070); the quieted soul is receptive; the restless soul cannot hear what silence carries. Conversely, the absence of peace impairs perception — "failing eyes" (Deu 28:65) is the somatic expression. The mechanism: peace as stillness creates the condition for perception; restlessness as noise prevents it. This is the hearing-direction of the silence vocabulary: peace enables the person to be still enough to perceive God. |
| OBS-117-T2-059 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | 1Ki 19:12, Psa 131:2 | The perception engagement reveals that peace is not only a passive condition (a state received) but an enabling posture — the person at peace is constitutionally equipped to perceive things they cannot hear when restless. The demamah threshold (1Ki 19:12) is analytically significant: the divine voice comes not in wind, earthquake, or fire (the dramatic) but in the still small voice — and only the person who has moved through silence can hear it. This means peace has a gnomic/perceptual consequence: the peace-bearing person perceives differently. Peace as the enabling condition for divine perception is a new finding. |
| OBS-117-T2-060 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Isa 26:3, Rom 8:6, Phili 4:7 | Yes — centrally. The cognitive engagement of peace is established in T2.4: Isa 26:3 (yetser — the formed mental disposition/thought, "whose mind is stayed on you"), Rom 8:6 (phronema — mind's settled orientation), Phili 4:7 (noema — thoughts guarded by peace). The cognitive faculty is both the condition for peace (mind stayed on God → peace) and the territory peace guards (peace guards thoughts/minds). Two-directional engagement: cognition produces peace when rightly directed; peace guards cognition when received. |
| OBS-117-T2-061 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Isa 32:17, Ecc 9:17, Pro 3:17 | Peace enables and deepens cognition. Evidence: (1) Isa 32:17 — "the effect of righteousness will be peace, and the result of righteousness, quietness and trust forever" — the sustained peace-condition produces trust which is the basis for deeper knowing; (2) Ecc 9:17 — "the words of the wise heard in quiet [nachat] are better than the shouting of a ruler among fools" — the quiet condition enables wisdom reception (prior session OBS-117-100 context); (3) The wisdom-peace correlation: Pro 3:17 ("all her paths are peace") — wisdom's paths produce peace; peace and wisdom are mutually enabling. Conversely: the restless inner state ("cannot be quiet," Isa 57:20 — prior session OBS-117-114 context) produces "mire and dirt" — cognitive disorder and moral opacity. Peace is cognitively enabling; restlessness impairs. |
| OBS-117-T2-062 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Isa 26:3, Ecc 9:17, Pro 3:17 | The cognition pattern establishes a reciprocal relationship: right cognition (mind stayed on God) generates peace; peace in turn enables deeper cognition (wisdom reception, trust-grounded knowing). This is not a simple linear chain but a reinforcing loop — the peaceful mind perceives more, knows more, trusts more, and thereby receives more peace. The implication for Session D: R100 (knowledge) and R163 (trust) are structurally connected to peace through this cognitive loop. |
| OBS-117-T2-063 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Lam 3:17, Isa 38:17 | Yes — in its negative form most directly. Lam 3:17 ("my soul is bereft of peace; I have forgotten what happiness is") — peace-deprivation produces the loss of remembered happiness. The person bereft of shalom cannot recall what flourishing was like — memory of wellbeing is inaccessible in the peaceless condition. This is a striking data point: peace's absence disrupts not only the present experience but the person's ability to remember peace. Conversely: Isa 38:17 ("it was for my welfare that I had great bitterness") — Hezekiah's retrospective memory of peace-through-suffering suggests that the person who has received peace carries a memory of its arrival that shapes their understanding of God. |
| OBS-117-T2-064 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Lam 3:17, Lam 3:21-22 | Peace-deprivation impairs memory (Lam 3:17 — forgotten happiness); peace-restoration enables retrospective memory of God's faithfulness. The Lam 3 context is particularly rich: the movement from Lam 3:17 ("forgotten") to Lam 3:21-22 ("but this I call to mind, and therefore I have hope: the steadfast love of the Lord never ceases") — the act of remembering God's hesed breaks through the peaceless condition and restores hope. The memory of God's covenant faithfulness is the cognitive act that enables the return to peace. Peace and memory have a mutual relationship: peace enables the memory of grace; the memory of grace enables the recovery of peace. |
| OBS-117-T2-065 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Lam 3:17, Lam 3:21-22 | The memory engagement reveals that peace has a temporal dimension — it exists in time, can be remembered, can be forgotten, and can be recovered through the act of remembering God's faithfulness. The Lam 3 pattern (forgotten → remembered → hope) shows that the memory of shalom is recoverable through deliberate recollection of covenant faithfulness, even from the depth of peacelessness. This connects to the T5 formation question: peace is not only a present condition but a remembered and hoped-for reality — it has a narrative arc in the person's experience. |
| OBS-117-T2-066 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Psa 4:8, Psa 35:27, Jer 30:10 | Yes — extensively. Peace is itself primarily an experiential/affective condition in many of its manifestations. The Emotion-Positive dimension assignments (groups 2508-001, 2509-001, 419-002, 418-004, 2585-001, 2585-002, and others) directly name the affective dimension. Key affective evidence: Psa 4:8 — lying down and sleeping in peace (the affect of deep security); Psa 35:27 — shout for joy and be glad, because of the Lord's delight in welfare (joy triggered by shalom); Jer 30:10 — "Jacob shall return and have quiet and ease, and none shall make him afraid" — ease as the affective state of the peacefully restored. The affect-peace connection is foundational: peace is, at the affective level, the felt condition of security, rest, welfare, and the absence of fear. |
| OBS-117-T2-067 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Judg 6:23, Phili 4:7, 1Sa 1:17 | Peace enables positive affect and resolves negative affect. Evidence: (1) Judg 6:23 — "Peace be to you. Do not fear" — divine peace directly displaces terror; (2) Est 9:22 — "sorrow turned into gladness and mourning into a holiday" (in the context of Purim rest/peace); (3) 1Sa 1:17-18 — Hannah went away and "her face was no longer sad" after Eli's peace-blessing; (4) Phili 4:7 — peace guards the heart against anxiety (Phili 4:6 context: "do not be anxious about anything... and the peace of God will guard"). Peace is the affective resolution of anxiety, fear, and sorrow. Its sustained presence does not bypass affect — peace-bearing people still weep, still feel — but it prevents affect from becoming debilitating or destructive. The person in peace can feel sorrow without being overwhelmed by it. |
| OBS-117-T2-068 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Judg 6:23, Lam 3:17, Phili 4:7 | The affective engagement reveals that peace is not emotional neutrality or the suppression of feeling but the condition in which affect functions rightly — neither overwhelming the person (anxiety, terror) nor being inaccessible to them (the peaceless Lam 3:17 — "forgotten what happiness is"). Peace is the affective ground: the person who has shalom can feel the full range of affect without being destroyed by negative affect or alienated from positive affect. This is a crucial distinction for the programme: peace is not the absence of feeling but the condition in which feeling is held and expressed rightly. |
| OBS-117-T2-069 | OBSERVATION | R117 peace |  | Silence on the creative faculty for peace is consistent with the registry's character: peace is not primarily described as a creative or generative condition but as a receptive, restful, and relational one. The data's silence on creativity suggests that the creative dimension of peace is not theologically foregrounded in the biblical vocabulary — the emphasis throughout is on reception, orientation, and the condition of the whole person rather than on creative output. This is worth noting as a gap for Session D if creativity's relationship to peace is to be explored. |
| OBS-117-T2-070 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Isa 30:15, Psa 34:14, Psa 120:7 | Yes — prominently. The volitional engagement of peace is one of its most theologically significant features. Evidence: (1) The refusal-of-peace pattern (Q&A-026): Isa 30:15 ("but you were unwilling") — the will actively refuses the peace God offers; Isa 28:12 ("they would not hear"), Jer 6:16 ("we will not walk in it") — peace is declined by volitional act; (2) The pursuit-of-peace pattern: Psa 34:14 ("seek peace and pursue it") — peace requires active volitional seeking; G1514 eirēneuō ("live peaceably with all") — the volitional act of choosing peace; (3) Psa 120:7 ("I am for peace") — the identity-level volitional commitment to peace as a fundamental choice of orientation; (4) H2790B group 408-001 — "holding silence in response to emotional provocation" — the volitional restraint of impulse in service of peace. Peace is not only received but chosen and refused. |
| OBS-117-T2-071 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Psa 34:14, Isa 26:3, Deu 29:19, Rom 12:18 | Peace both requires and enables volition: (1) Requires volition: the pursuit of peace (Psa 34:14), the choice to be at peace with others (Rom 12:18 — G1514), the volitional orientation of the mind toward God (Isa 26:3) — all are deliberate acts of will; (2) Enables volition: the quieted person is in a better position to choose rightly — H2790B group 408-001 (holding silence under emotional provocation) shows peace-as-restraint enabling volition over impulse; H2814 group 409-004 (prophetic inner resolve, refusing silence for God's purposes) shows the person of peace being more decisively volitional, not less; (3) False peace bypasses true volition: Deu 29:19 — the self-deceived person's volitional claim ("I shall be safe") is not a genuine act of will directed toward peace but a bypass of the real volitional cost of covenant faithfulness. Peace-bearing volition is the will rightly informed and directed; false peace is the will disengaged from reality. |
| OBS-117-T2-072 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Isa 30:15, Psa 34:14, Psa 120:7 | The volitional pattern reveals that peace is not a deterministic state but a characteristic that engages the will at every point — in its reception (choosing to orient toward God), its pursuit (actively seeking peace with others), its refusal (the structural human refusal documented in the prophets), and its false claim (bypassing the real volitional cost). This has significant anthropological implications: the human person is not a passive recipient of peace but an active participant in whether and how peace is received, held, and expressed. The refusal pattern (Isa 30:15) is the most theologically significant volitional evidence — God's offer of peace is genuine, the refusal is genuine, and the consequence is the person's responsibility. |
| OBS-117-T2-073 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Jam 3:18, Col 1:20, Est 10:3 | Yes — peace both expresses and enables agency. Evidence: (1) Peacemaking as an act of agency: Jam 3:18 ("a harvest of righteousness is sown in peace by those who make peace") — peacemakers are agents who do something in the world through their peace; (2) Mordecai (Est 10:3, OBS-117-071 from prior session) — "sought the welfare of his people and spoke peace to all his people" — the sha.lom orientation expressed as leadership agency; (3) G1517 eirēnopoieō (Col 1:20 — "making peace by the blood of his cross") — Christ as the supreme agent of peace; (4) The prophetic refusal of silence for advocacy (H2814 group 409-004) — the person of peace choosing to speak when silence would be complicit. The peacemaker (Matt 5:9 — not in anchor verses but in the eirēnē vocabulary's scope) is explicitly an agent who does something. |
| OBS-117-T2-076 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Isa 48:22, Mal 2:6, Isa 59:8, Deu 29:19 | Yes — extensively. Peace has a moral evaluation function: the presence or absence of shalom is a moral indicator. Evidence: (1) Isa 48:22, 57:21 — "there is no peace for the wicked" — moral condition is the determinative factor for peace's availability; (2) Isa 59:8 — "the way of peace they do not know" — moral disorder produces cognitive/perceptual blindness to peace's way; (3) Mal 2:6 — "walked with me in peace and uprightness" — shalom paired with uprightness as the moral-character portrait; (4) The false-peace pattern (Jer 6:14, Deu 29:19) — the moral faculty has been corrupted when false peace is claimed. Peace's connection to moral evaluation is among the richest in the programme: the peaceful person evaluates rightly; the wicked person cannot perceive peace's way; the self-deceived person evaluates falsely. |
| OBS-117-T2-077 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Isa 26:3, Deu 29:19, Jer 6:14, Isa 57:20 | Peace enables moral evaluation; its absence and its false form impair it. (1) Enabling: the quiet person evaluates rightly (Pro 14:33 — "wisdom rests in the heart of a man of understanding... but it makes itself known even in the midst of fools" — associated with the quiet-wisdom vocabulary); the peaceful mind (Isa 26:3) is in a better position to discern rightly; (2) False peace impairs: Deu 29:19 — the stubborn heart claiming false shalom "bypasses" the moral evaluation faculty — the person evaluates their own condition falsely because they are in the false-peace condition; Jer 6:14 — the prophets who cry "Peace, peace" have corrupted their moral evaluative function; (3) Absence impairs: Isa 57:20 ("the wicked are like the tossing sea... tossing up mire and dirt") — the restless inner state produces moral opacity, not discernment. |
| OBS-117-T2-078 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Isa 26:3, Deu 29:19 | The moral evaluation pattern reveals that peace is both the condition that enables rightly-ordered moral judgment and itself a moral diagnostic tool. The person whose inner life is genuinely at peace is in a position to evaluate moral reality clearly; the person who has claimed false peace is systematically unable to see their condition rightly (Deu 29:19 — "I shall be safe" is the self-description of the self-deceived). This makes peace not only a moral characteristic but an epistemological one in the moral domain — the state of one's peace is a condition of moral perception. |
| OBS-117-T2-079 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Psa 32:3-5, Deu 29:19 | Yes — the connection between peace and conscience is among the most analytically significant in the registry. The primary evidence: (1) Psa 32:3-5 — "when I kept silent, my bones wasted away through my groaning all day long... your hand was heavy upon me" (prior session OBS-117-076 context): suppressed confession = withheld inner acknowledgment of sin = somatic deterioration. The sequence: conscience pressing → person suppressing → bones wasting → person confessing → peace restored (Psa 32:5 — "I acknowledged my sin... and you forgave"). The conscience is the inner faculty whose suppression destroys peace and whose exercise restores it; (2) H2814 group 409-001 — "inward suppression of speech under emotional or spiritual pressure, with deteriorating inner consequence" — conscience-suppression as a pattern; (3) 2509-002 (Deu 29:19) — the false shalom claimed against the conscience's witness; the conscience is what the stubborn heart overrides to claim false peace. |
| OBS-117-T2-080 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Psa 32:3-5, Deu 29:19 | Three conscience-patterns: (1) Suppressed conscience impairs peace (the primary direction): Psa 32:3 — suppressed conscience → bones wasting, no peace; the relationship is causal — withheld confession prevents peace; (2) Exercised conscience enables peace: Psa 32:5 — acknowledged sin → forgiveness → relief; the confession that exercises conscience leads to peace as its outcome; (3) False peace bypasses conscience: Deu 29:19 — the stubborn heart generates false peace by bypassing the conscience's witness; this is the bypass pattern — false shalom is what remains when conscience is overridden. The conscience is thus both the gateway to peace (through confession) and the faculty whose suppression most directly destroys peace (Psa 32:3). No other inner faculty has this bidirectional gateway function as clearly documented in the data. |
| OBS-117-T2-081 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Psa 32:3-5, Deu 29:19 | The conscience pattern reveals that peace and conscience are structurally interdependent: genuine peace requires a functioning and exercised conscience; the suppressed conscience is the primary internal mechanism of peacelessness; and the false peace that bypasses conscience is the most dangerous corruption of the characteristic. Psa 32 is the governing text: the person cannot have peace while suppressing conscience, and when conscience is exercised (confession), peace becomes available. This is a new finding: conscience is the gateway faculty for peace — more than any other inner faculty, the state of the conscience determines whether genuine peace is constitutionally accessible. |
| OBS-117-T2-082 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Mal 2:6, Jer 29:7 | Yes. Conscientiousness as the integration of moral awareness, volition, and action is precisely what the peace-as-character data describes. Evidence: (1) Mal 2:6 — Levi "walked with me in peace and uprightness, and turned many from iniquity" — the three elements: moral awareness (uprightness), volitional commitment (walking with God), and consequential action (turning many from iniquity) are all present in the portrait of the peaceable priest; (2) Jer 29:7 — "seek the welfare of the city... pray to the Lord on its behalf, for in its welfare you will find your welfare" — conscience (awareness of responsibility), volition (seek, pray), and action (community engagement) integrated in the peace-orientation; (3) H2790B group 408-001 — holding silence under emotional provocation — the conscientious restraint of impulse in the service of peace. Conscientiousness is the enacted form of the peace-as-moral-character dimension. |
| OBS-117-T2-085 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | 1Ch 12:17, Rom 12:18, Eph 2:14, Jam 3:18 | Yes — centrally. Peace is among the most relationally engaged characteristics in the programme. Evidence spans multiple dimensions: (1) Friendship bond (H7965J, 2510-001) — shalom as the inner relational bond of loyalty and heart-union (1Ch 12:17); (2) Greeting-blessing (H7965K, 2511-001) — the invocation of peace as the act of goodwill toward another; (3) Covenant peace (2508-002) — the relational condition between God and his people; (4) Peacemaking (G1517, Jam 3:18) — the capacity to make peace between parties; (5) G1514 eirēneuō — actively being at peace with all (Rom 12:18); (6) Eph 2:14-16 — Christ making peace between Jew and Gentile, creating one body. The relational dimension of peace is not peripheral but constitutive — the peace vocabulary is inherently directed toward-another. |
| OBS-117-T2-086 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Eph 2:14, 1Ch 12:17, Jer 29:7, Gen 37:4 | Authentic peace deeply enables and deepens relational capacity. Evidence: (1) 1Ch 12:17 — the sha.lom-friendship produces heart-union; (2) Eph 2:14-16 — Christ's peace breaks down the "dividing wall of hostility" and creates one body from two peoples — peace is the engine of the most dramatic relational restoration in the NT; (3) Jer 29:7 — seeking the welfare of the exile city requires expanding relational capacity to include the former enemy's community; (4) 1Sa 25:6 (2511-001) — the greeting-blessing as an act of relational goodwill. Conversely: peacelessness impairs relational capacity — Gen 37:4 ("they hated him and could not speak peacefully to him") — the absence of sha.lom-orientation makes genuine relational connection impossible; Jer 20:10 (anshei sha.lom who betray) — false sha.lom destroys relational capacity through betrayal. The instruction note for T3.11 distinguishes characteristics that build/sustain connection from those that restore/repair it — this registry does both: shalom builds connection (friendship, greeting, covenant) and restores broken connection (Eph 2:14). |
| OBS-117-T2-087 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Psa 120:7, Eph 2:14, 1Ch 12:17 | The relational pattern reveals that peace is constitutionally relational — it cannot be fully described as an inner private condition without reference to the other toward whom it is directed. The "I am for peace" (Psa 120:7) is not merely an inner statement but a relational declaration against the other party's war-orientation. The covenant of peace (2508-002) is inherently two-party. The friendship bond (2510-001) is inherently toward-another. The peace that guards the heart (Phili 4:7) is received in the context of prayer and petition to God. The conclusion: peace is both inner and relational simultaneously — the inner condition and the relational orientation are not separable dimensions but two faces of the same reality. The interiority and the relationality of peace are co-constitutive. |
| OBS-117-T2-088 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Num 6:26, Joh 14:27, Gal 5:22, Psa 85:8 | Yes — this is the dominant direction in the peace corpus. The God-to-human operation is extensively documented: (1) Covenant gift: Num 6:26 ("the Lord give you peace" — Aaronic blessing), Isa 54:10 ("my covenant of peace shall not be removed"), Jer 29:11 ("plans for welfare and not for evil"); (2) God speaking peace: Psa 85:8 ("he will speak peace to his people, to his saints"); (3) Christ-given peace: Joh 14:27 ("my peace I give to you"); (4) Spirit-given peace: Gal 5:22 (Spirit-fruit); (5) God making well-being: Isa 45:7 ("I make well-being"); (6) The God of peace: Rom 15:33, 16:20, Phili 4:9, 1Th 5:23 — God's identity as "the God of peace" who acts toward the human person. The God-to-human direction is established at every level: covenant, speech, person of Christ, Spirit, and title. |
| OBS-117-T2-089 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Num 25:12, Isa 54:10, Col 1:20, Joh 14:27 | The data establishes covenant as the primary basis. God's peace is extended: (1) Through covenant: Num 25:12 ("my covenant of peace"), Isa 54:10 (covenant of peace that shall not be removed), Eze 34:25, 37:26 (covenants of peace in the new covenant context); (2) Through covenant faithfulness: Psa 85:8 — God speaks peace to his saints "but let them not turn back to folly" — the condition is covenant fidelity, not merit; (3) Through the cross: Col 1:20 ("making peace by the blood of his cross") — the basis shifts in the NT from the Sinai covenant to the new covenant accomplished in Christ's sacrifice; (4) Through grace not merit: Joh 14:27 ("not as the world gives do I give to you") — the basis of Christ's peace-gift is explicitly distinguished from transactional exchange; the gift is given freely. The ground shifts from covenant-as-obligation to covenant-as-grace across the canon. |
| OBS-117-T2-090 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Psa 35:27, Jer 29:11, Judg 6:24 | God's extension of peace reveals his constitutive orientation of goodwill and wholeness toward the human person. Three dimensions: (1) God delights in the welfare of his servant (Psa 35:27 — OBS-117-149) — peace-giving is the expression of divine delight, not merely duty; (2) God plans for welfare and not for evil (Jer 29:11) — the divine intention toward the human person is purposively peaceful; (3) God identified as peace (YHWH-Shalom, 406-001) — God's own identity includes peace as a constitutive attribute, meaning his extension of peace is not situational but essential to who he is. The convergence of these three dimensions reveals that God's peace-giving is not a response to human deserving but the overflow of his own identity — the God who IS peace gives peace because that is what he is. |
| OBS-117-T2-091 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Isa 27:5, Mal 2:6, Isa 30:15, Psa 62:5 | Yes — several forms. (1) Making peace with God: Isa 27:5 ("let them make peace with me, let them make peace with me") — the human act of coming into covenant relationship with God; (2) Walking with God in peace: Mal 2:6 — the sustained human posture of covenant faithfulness expressed as peace-with-God; (3) Returning to God: Isa 30:15 ("in returning and rest you shall be saved") — returning to God as the peace-generating human act (even though the peace itself is God's gift, the returning is the human movement toward God); (4) Praying for peace: Psa 122:6 ("pray for the peace of Jerusalem") — intercession as the human peace-orientation toward God on behalf of community; (5) The soul waiting in silence (Psa 62:5 — 2585-001) — "for God alone, O my soul, wait in silence" — the posture of the soul directed toward God in peaceful expectation. |
| OBS-117-T2-092 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Isa 26:3, Isa 30:15, Psa 4:8 | The data is specific about the inner posture required: (1) Trust: Isa 26:3 ("because he trusts in you" → perfect peace); Psa 4:8 ("for you alone, O Lord, make me dwell in safety" — trust as the inner posture); (2) Returning / repentance: Isa 30:15 ("in returning and rest you shall be saved") — the inner orientation toward God involves turning from the previous direction; (3) Quietness: Isa 30:15 ("in quietness and in trust shall be your strength") — the inner condition of stillness is required before the movement toward God can be genuinely receptive; (4) Non-stubbornness: Deu 29:19 reverse — the stubborn heart is the posture that prevents movement toward God; Isa 30:15 ("but you were unwilling") — the refusal is the polar opposite of the required posture. The required inner posture: quiet trust, returning orientation, and non-stubborn receptivity. |
| OBS-117-T2-093 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Isa 30:15, Isa 26:3, Psa 85:8 | The human-to-God movement in peace reveals that the human person has the constitutional capacity to orient toward God and thereby receive peace — the movement is not coerced but voluntary, and it is genuinely possible. The refusal pattern (Isa 30:15 — "but you were unwilling") demonstrates that the movement can be declined — the human person retains the capacity to refuse peace. This reveals: the human's relational standing with God is not static but dynamically engaged by the direction of the inner person's orientation. When the human turns toward God in quiet trust, peace flows from God to the human; when the human turns away (stubbornness, false peace), the peace-channel is closed from the human side. God remains ready to give peace; the human's inner posture determines whether it is received. |
| OBS-117-T2-094 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | 1Sa 25:6, Jer 29:7, Jam 3:18, Rom 12:18 | Yes — extensively. Human-to-human extension of peace: (1) The greeting-blessing (H7965K, 2511-001): 1Sa 25:6 — the triple sha.lom blessing extended by messengers to Nabal; (2) Seeking the welfare of others: Est 10:3 (Mordecai seeking the sha.lom of his people), Jer 29:7 (seeking the welfare of the exile city for others' benefit); (3) Peacemaking: Jam 3:18 — the peacemaker sows righteousness in peace; (4) G1514 eirēneuō: Rom 12:18 — "as far as it depends on you, live peaceably with all"; (5) Apostolic peace-blessing: Rom 1:7 and all Pauline letter openings — "grace and peace from God our Father and the Lord Jesus Christ" — the apostle extending peace in blessing; (6) Speaking peace: Psa 122:8 — "for my brothers and companions' sake I will say, 'Peace be within you'" — peace spoken toward community. |
| OBS-117-T2-095 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Psa 120:7, Jer 29:7, Jam 3:17 | The data establishes several inner conditions for genuine peace-giving: (1) Having received peace: the apostolic blessing (Rom 1:7) is the outflow of the apostle's own experience of grace-and-peace from God; one extends what one has received — Joh 14:27's "my peace I give to you" is the paradigm: Christ gives what he IS; (2) Authentic inner orientation toward peace: Psa 120:7 ("I am for peace") — the person who genuinely extends peace is one whose fundamental orientation IS peace; the one whose heart is toward war cannot genuinely extend peace (Psa 28:3 — speaking peace while evil is in the heart is its corruption); (3) Seeking the other's welfare: Jer 29:7 — the extension of peace to the exile city requires an inner orientation of care for those who might be regarded as enemies; (4) Wisdom: G1516 eirēnikos — peaceableness is the fruit of heavenly wisdom (Jam 3:17) — wisdom is the inner condition enabling peaceful extension. |
| OBS-117-T2-096 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Heb 12:11, Psa 120:7, Jer 29:7 | The data implies that genuine peace-extension requires prior reception and formation: (1) Prior reception: the person who has not received peace cannot authentically give it — the greeting-blessing (1Sa 25:6) is an invocation of God's peace upon another, not a self-generated gift; the extension is mediated from the divine source; (2) Character formation: G1516 eirēnikos — peaceableness as quality of heavenly wisdom and of discipline (Heb 12:11 — "the peaceful fruit of righteousness to those who have been trained by it") — the person who extends peace has been trained; (3) Identity-level commitment: Psa 120:7 ("I am for peace") — the giver of peace is a person whose identity is peace; the extension flows from being, not merely from choosing. The data does not require perfect peace in the giver — Jer 29:7 (exiles seeking the welfare of their captor city) shows peace-extension is possible even from a position of suffering — but it requires genuine orientation. |
| OBS-117-T2-097 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Heb 12:11, Isa 54:13, 1Sa 25:6 | Yes — but the data for human-from-human reception is less explicit than for God-from-human or human-to-human giving. Evidence: (1) The greeting-blessing: the recipients of the sha.lom blessing (1Sa 25:6's addressees) receive peace from the messenger; (2) The apostolic blessing-recipients: every Pauline letter recipient receives "grace and peace" in the greeting — the community receives peace through the apostolic proclamation; (3) G1516 — the discipline produces eirēnikos fruit in "those who have been trained by it" (Heb 12:11) — the person who submits to a trainer/community receives peace as a fruit of that relationship; (4) The woman healed in Luk 8:48 — "go in peace" — she receives peace from Christ's word; (5) Children of Zion taught by God: Isa 54:13 ("great shall be the peace of your children") — the covenant community's children receive peace through being taught. |
| OBS-117-T2-098 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Psa 131:2, 1Ch 12:17, Gen 37:4, Deu 29:19 | Enabling conditions: (1) Receptivity — the quieted person is positioned to receive what the peaceful person extends (Psa 131:2 — the weaned-child posture of receptive calm); (2) Genuine seeking: 1Ch 12:17 — "if you have come to me in friendship to help me" — the intention that enables the sha.lom relationship is genuine orientation toward the other's good; (3) Non-hostility: Gen 37:4 — the brothers "could not speak peaceably to Joseph" because of hatred — hatred blocks peace-reception. Blocking conditions: (1) Hatred: Gen 37:4; (2) Stubbornness: Isa 30:15 — the stubborn heart refuses peace even when God offers it; human stubbornness would equally block human-to-human peace-reception; (3) False peace already in place: Deu 29:19 — the person who has self-generated false peace is not available to receive genuine peace from another because they believe they already have it. |
| OBS-117-T2-099 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Isa 30:15, Jer 6:16, Jer 4:10 | The person who encounters peace and does not receive it is in the refusal pattern documented at Isa 30:15 and Jer 6:16. Their inner state: (1) Continuing inner restlessness — they do not find the rest they were offered; (2) Moral hardness — stubbornness persists as the inner posture (Isa 30:15 — "but you were unwilling"); (3) Compounding peacelessness — the refusal of offered peace does not leave the person where they were; the sword reaches their life (Jer 4:10) even after the prophetic promise of peace was extended. The inner-being consequence of refusing offered peace: the condition deteriorates rather than remaining static. This is theologically significant — peace refused is not neutral; it worsens the peaceless condition. The person who encounters peace from another and rejects it is not unaffected by the encounter. |
| OBS-117-T2-100 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Isa 54:10, Psa 41:9, Eph 2:14, Jer 29:7 | Yes — the data shows different modes of operation. Within existing covenant relationship: God's covenant of peace (Isa 54:10) is addressed to those already in covenant with him — it deepens and confirms the existing relationship. Within existing friendship: H7965J (2510-001) — the sha.lom-friendship bond is most dramatically revealed when it is violated (Psa 41:9, Jer 20:10) — the betrayal is devastating precisely because the relationship was pre-existing. In new relationships: G1514 eirēneuō (Rom 12:18 — "with all") — the call to peace extends to all people, including strangers; Jer 29:7 — seeking the welfare of the exile city involves extending peace to a new (and hostile) community. Eph 2:14 — Christ making peace between Jew and Gentile is the formation of a new relationship between previously hostile parties. The data does not show a sharp distinction in the characteristic itself — peace is offered in both contexts — but the vulnerability in existing relationships (betrayal wounding) and the cost in new relationships (hostile parties) are different. |
| OBS-117-T2-101 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Psa 85:8, Jer 29:7, Eph 2:14, Col 1:20 | Both — with a clear movement across the canon from primarily covenantal to universally-extended. OT: most explicit peace-giving is within covenant (Num 6:26 — to Israel; Isa 54:10 — to the covenant people; Psa 85:8 — to "his saints"). However: Jer 29:7 (seeking welfare of the exile city), Isa 52:7/Nah 1:15 (peace proclaimed to nations), Zec 9:10 (messianic king speaking peace to the nations) — peace crosses covenantal boundaries in the OT prophets. NT: Eph 2:14-16 — Christ makes peace between Jew and Gentile, creating one body; G1515 group 426-002 — apostolic peace-blessing in every letter extends to Gentile recipients; Col 1:20 ("all things, whether on earth or in heaven") — the scope of cosmic peace-making is universal. The trajectory: covenant-primary → covenant-expansive → universally offered through Christ. |
| OBS-117-T2-102 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Isa 48:22, Isa 57:19, Eph 2:14, Col 1:20 | The data establishes both inclusion and exclusion: (1) Exclusion: "there is no peace for the wicked" (Isa 48:22, 57:21) — moral condition determines peace-access; (2) Conditional exclusion: Psa 85:8 — "but let them not turn back to folly" — covenant-unfaithfulness risks peace; Deu 23:6 — "you shall not seek their peace" for Ammonites/Moabites — historical covenant boundaries; (3) Expanding inclusion: Isa 57:19 — "peace, peace to the far and to the near" — peace offered to those formerly excluded; Isa 52:7 — the herald of peace to the nations; Eph 2:14 — Gentiles included through Christ. The trajectory of scope is progressive expansion: from Israel → to the nations → to all peoples in Christ → to all things (Col 1:20). The only sustained exclusion in the data is moral: "there is no peace for the wicked." The covenantal exclusions are overcome in Christ. |

| OBS-117-T2-105 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Dan 10:19 | Yes — Dan 10:19 establishes angelic mediation of peace directly: "peace be with you; be strong and of good courage" — the angelic being speaks peace to Daniel as a constitutive part of strengthening him for the vision. The angelic mediation of peace is not merely formal (announcing) but apparently effective (Daniel's fear is addressed and he is strengthened). |
| OBS-117-T2-106 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | 1Sa 1:17, Isa 38:17, Eph 2:14, Gal 5:22 | Yes — peace produces transformation at multiple levels: (1) Inner condition change: 1Sa 1:17-18 — Hannah departs after the blessing and "her face was no longer sad" — the peace-word transforms her inner emotional state immediately; (2) Orientation change: Isa 26:3 — the mind's orientation toward God produces perfect peace, which then shapes how the person views all of reality; (3) Self-understanding: Isa 38:17 — Hezekiah's retrospective ("it was for my welfare that I had great bitterness") — the receipt of peace through suffering transforms his self-understanding of the suffering; suffering is reread as the path to welfare; (4) Relational transformation: Eph 2:14-16 — Christ's peace breaks down the dividing wall, producing new corporate identity (one body); formerly hostile groups are transformed into one; (5) Character transformation: Gal 5:22 — Spirit-produced peace forms the person's character over time. |
| OBS-117-T2-107 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Jer 16:5, Isa 54:10, Eph 2:14 | The data shows both: (1) Reversible: Jer 16:5 ("I have taken away my peace from this people") — God can withdraw peace, reversing the relational condition; Psa 85:8 ("let them not turn back to folly") — the covenant condition of peace can be forfeited through turning; the human person can move from peace to peacelessness through rebellion; (2) Irreversible (covenantal): Isa 54:10 ("my covenant of peace shall not be removed") — the eschatological covenant of peace is explicitly declared irrevocable by God's unilateral commitment; (3) Irreversible (accomplished): Eph 2:14-16 — Christ's peace-making through the cross is a completed historical act; the dividing wall has been broken down — this cannot be re-erected from the human side. The data distinguishes between experiential peace (reversible, depending on human orientation and divine response to sin) and covenantal/accomplished peace (irreversible, grounded in God's identity and Christ's cross). |
| OBS-117-T2-108 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Lam 3:17-26, Isa 30:15, Psa 32:3-5, Heb 12:11 | Yes — the data documents several sequences: (1) Lam 3 sequence: soul bereft of peace (3:17) → remembering God's hesed (3:21-22) → hope restored (3:24) → quiet waiting (3:26) — the sequence from peaceless despair through memory to hope through waiting; (2) Isa 30:15 sequence: returning → rest → quietness → trust → strength/salvation — a five-stage sequence where each stage enables the next; (3) Psa 32 sequence: sin → suppressed conscience → somatic deterioration → confession → divine forgiveness → peace; (4) Gift-to-fruit sequence: God gives peace (Num 6:26) → received in the mind/heart (Isa 26:3) → enacted in the body (Psa 4:8) → expressed in the community (Rom 12:18) — the reception-to-expression sequence; (5) Formation sequence: discipline → trained → peaceful fruit (Heb 12:11) — the formative sequence through suffering into peace. |
| OBS-117-T2-109 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Lam 3:17-26, Isa 30:15, Heb 12:11 | The sequences converge on three structural insights: (1) Peace is not arrived at directly but through precondition states — trust, returning, quietness, confession, discipline are all precondition stages that enable peace's reception; (2) Peace received produces enabling states — strength, hope, stability, and community expression flow from peace received; peace is both an end-state (received) and an enabling-state (productive); (3) The Lam 3 sequence is the most instructive for the peaceless person: the route from peacelessness back to peace is through memory of God's faithfulness (not through achieving peace directly). The sequence reveals that peace has a pathway structure — it is neither instantaneous (always) nor arbitrary in how it comes and goes. |
| OBS-117-T2-110 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Judg 6:23, Psa 32:5, Isa 26:3, Heb 12:11, Col 1:20, Gal 5:22 | The data identifies multiple mechanisms: (1) Divine word/proclamation: Judg 6:23 ("Peace be to you") — the spoken word of peace from God or his messenger produces the inner shift from terror to peace; Joh 14:27 ("my peace I give to you") — Christ's word-gift; the proclamation is a mechanism; (2) Conscience-activation: Psa 32:3-5 — the mechanism is confession activating what suppressed conscience had blocked; the act of acknowledgment opens the peace-channel; (3) Cognitive re-direction: Isa 26:3 — the mechanism is the deliberate directing of the mind toward God; (4) Discipline and training: Heb 12:11 — the mechanism is the sustained experience of discipline by a trainer, producing peace as fruit over time; (5) Cross-accomplishment: Col 1:20 — the mechanism is Christ's blood of the cross making peace; a historical accomplished act; (6) Spirit indwelling: Gal 5:22 — the Spirit's presence produces peace as fruit through the mechanism of indwelling and progressive formation. |
| OBS-117-T2-111 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Judg 6:23, Psa 32:5, Isa 26:3, Heb 12:11, Col 1:20 | Yes — the mechanisms differ by context: (1) Crisis/encounter context: divine word is the immediate mechanism (Judg 6:23, Dan 10:19, Joh 14:27) — peace comes through an encounter with divine presence or word; (2) Moral failure context: conscience-activation and confession are the mechanism (Psa 32:3-5) — the route back from peacelessness through sin is through honest acknowledgment; (3) Cognitive orientation context: sustained mind-direction is the mechanism (Isa 26:3, Rom 8:6) — for the ongoing inner condition of peace, the mechanism is daily cognitive orientation; (4) Formative context: discipline over time is the mechanism (Heb 12:11) — peace-as-character is formed through sustained training rather than instantaneous receipt; (5) Eschatological/cosmic context: Christ's cross is the mechanism (Col 1:20, Isa 53:5) — for the foundational peace-with-God and cosmic peace, the mechanism is unique historical accomplishment. No single mechanism covers all contexts. |
| OBS-117-T2-112 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Isa 53:5, Isa 38:17, Heb 12:11, Lam 3:17 | Yes — and the peace-suffering relationship is one of the most analytically significant in the entire registry. Evidence: (1) Isa 53:5 — "upon him was the chastisement that brought us peace, and with his wounds we are healed" — peace purchased through the Servant's suffering; (2) Isa 38:17 — "it was for my welfare that I had great bitterness" — Hezekiah's welfare arrived through his suffering; (3) Heb 12:11 — "for the moment all discipline seems painful rather than pleasant, but later it yields the peaceful fruit of righteousness" — peace is the outcome of endured discipline (suffering); (4) Lam 3:17 — "my soul is bereft of peace" — the peaceless condition in suffering; (5) 2Cor 2:13 — Paul's spirit not at rest amid his afflictions — the tension between suffering and peace; (6) 2Th 1:7 — "relief [anesis] to you who are afflicted... when the Lord Jesus is revealed" — eschatological peace as relief from present suffering. |
| OBS-117-T2-113 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Heb 12:11, Isa 38:17, Lam 3:17, Phili 4:7 | All four relationships appear: (1) Produces: Heb 12:11 — discipline produces peaceful fruit in those trained; Isa 38:17 — bitterness was the path to welfare; suffering is the generative mechanism in certain contexts; (2) Tests: Lam 3:17 — the soul bereft of peace is the person whose peace is tested to its limit; the sustained peacelessness under catastrophe tests whether the person's peace has deep roots or is situational; (3) Reveals: the person who maintains peace through suffering reveals a peace grounded in God rather than circumstance (Phili 4:7 — "surpasses all understanding" suggests it transcends circumstantial explanation); (4) Deepens: the retrospective memory of peace-through-suffering (Isa 38:17) deepens understanding of God's ways; suffering that produces peace creates a richer experiential knowledge of shalom than suffering-free peace. The paradox of T1 (Q&A-023) is confirmed here: suffering is not peace's enemy but its formative companion in the data. |

| OBS-117-T2-115 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | 1Th 5:23, Gal 5:22, Heb 12:11 | Peace plays a dual role in sanctification: (1) Condition for sanctification: 1Th 5:23 — the God of peace sanctifies; the peace-ground is the condition under which sanctification proceeds; (2) Fruit of sanctification: Gal 5:22 (Spirit-fruit), Heb 12:11 (fruit of discipline) — peace is what sanctification produces. This dual role (condition and fruit) makes peace both the environment of growth and its outcome — uniquely integrative. The implication: a person cannot be sanctified while persistently refusing peace (the refusal-pattern of Isa 30:15 is simultaneously a refusal of the conditions for sanctification); and when sanctification proceeds, peace is among its visible fruits. |

| OBS-117-T2-120 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Isa 26:3, Isa 32:17, Psa 32:5, Isa 30:15 | Multiple sequential relationships are documented: (1) Trust → peace (peace follows trust): Isa 26:3 — "because he trusts in you" → perfect peace; Psa 4:8 — "you alone... make me dwell in safety" → "in peace I will lie down"; trust consistently precedes the peace condition; (2) Peace → strength: Isa 30:15 — "in quietness and in trust shall be your strength" — peace-quietness produces strength as its fruit; (3) Righteousness → peace (peace follows righteousness): Isa 32:17 — "the effect of righteousness will be peace"; Jam 3:18 — "sown in peace by those who make peace... a harvest of righteousness" — the sequence is bidirectional: righteousness produces peace and peace is the condition for sowing righteousness; (4) Peace → joy: Psa 35:27 — shout for joy in response to shalom-welfare; Isa 55:12 — "you shall go out in joy and be led forth in peace" — here peace accompanies and enables joy; (5) Confession → peace: Psa 32:5 — confession → forgiveness → relief; conscience-exercise enables peace. |
| OBS-117-T2-121 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Isa 26:3, Isa 32:17, Isa 30:15 | The sequences are predominantly causal: (1) Trust → peace is explicitly causal: Isa 26:3 ("because he trusts in you" — causal clause); (2) Peace → strength is explicitly causal: Isa 30:15 ("in quietness and in trust shall be your strength" — instrumentally causal); (3) Righteousness → peace is causal: Isa 32:17 ("the effect of righteousness" — effect = causal output); (4) Confession → peace (Psa 32) is causal: confession removes the suppression blocking peace; (5) Peace → joy appears correlational or mutually enabling rather than strictly causal — joy accompanies peace without being mechanically produced by it. The primary sequences are causal in both directions: trust produces peace; peace produces strength; this creates a reinforcing system where trusting more deeply produces more peace, which produces more strength, which enables more trust. |
| OBS-117-T2-122 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Isa 30:15, Jam 3:18, Psa 119:165 | Yes — documented: (1) Peace produces strength: Isa 30:15 — "in quietness and in trust shall be your strength"; peace-quietness is instrumentally causal for strength; (2) Peace produces joy: Psa 35:27 — shout for joy because the Lord delights in the servant's welfare; Isa 55:12 — peace accompanies joy in the restoration; (3) Peace produces righteousness: Jam 3:18 — "sown in peace... a harvest of righteousness"; the peace-condition enables the sowing that produces righteousness; (4) Peace produces stability: Psa 119:165 — "great peace have those who love your law; nothing can make them stumble" — peace produces stumble-proof stability; (5) Peace produces receptivity: the silence-dimension of peace enables wisdom reception (Ecc 9:17 — the wise heard in quiet); peace creates the condition for receiving other goods. |
| OBS-117-T2-123 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Isa 26:3, Isa 32:17, Psa 32:5, Heb 12:11, Gal 5:22 | Yes — documented: (1) Trust produces peace: Isa 26:3 (causal — "because he trusts in you"), Psa 4:8; (2) Righteousness produces peace: Isa 32:17 ("the effect of righteousness will be peace"); (3) Confession produces peace: Psa 32:5 (confession → forgiveness → peace); (4) Discipline produces peace: Heb 12:11 (training → peaceful fruit); (5) The Spirit produces peace: Gal 5:22 (Spirit-fruit); (6) Wisdom produces peace: Pro 3:17 ("all her paths are peace"); (7) Love of God's law produces peace: Psa 119:165 ("great peace have those who love your law"). This confirms that peace is not a simple direct gift but a multi-factored condition — multiple inner and relational realities contribute to its presence. |
| OBS-117-T2-124 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Gal 5:22, Pro 3:17, Col 1:20, Eph 2:14 | Peace appears to be a constituent of several larger realities: (1) Shalom is a constituent of the Spirit-fruit cluster (Gal 5:22) — peace is one element within the multi-characteristic Spirit-production; it is not the whole but a part; (2) Peace is a constituent of wisdom's path (Pro 3:17) — "all her paths are peace" makes peace the characteristic quality of wisdom's way; (3) Peace is constituted by covenant: the covenant of peace (Isa 54:10) makes peace a product of the larger covenantal structure. Conversely: the characteristic of reconciliation (R130) appears to constitute peace at the structural level — Col 1:20 (making peace through reconciliation) and Eph 2:14-16 (peace = the breaking down of the dividing wall = reconciliation) suggest that reconciliation is the mechanism that constitutes relational peace. This is the clearest constitutive relationship in the data: reconciliation constitutes relational peace; peace is reconciliation's experiential outcome. |

| OBS-117-T2-126 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Isa 54:10, Num 25:12 | Yes — the SHALOM root (sha.lem = completeness, sha.lam = to complete/ally, sha.lom = peace) generates both peace (R117) and covenant-completion vocabulary (R34 — sha.lam to ally, sha.lem to be complete). The root-level architecture reveals that peace and covenant-completion are generated from the same root: shalom is constitutively a completeness-word, and the covenantal vocabulary (completeness, allied, full payment) shares the same semantic base. This root-level sharing is the deepest structural evidence for the peace-covenant relationship. |

| OBS-117-T2-128 | OBSERVATION | R117 peace | Gal 5:22, Isa 55:12, Deu 28:65 | The closest neighbours requiring distinction work: (1) Rest (nuach/menuchah — major component of R117 itself): rest and peace co-reside in this registry, which creates an internal distinction challenge. Rest is the cessation-of-striving dimension; peace is the positive wholeness-condition that rest may produce or accompany. Rest can be given and withdrawn (Deu 28:65 — no resting place); peace encompasses more than the cessation of striving (shalom includes welfare, friendship, covenant, and messianic embodiment). Rest is a component of peace's semantic range but not co-extensive with it; (2) Joy (R97, 21 shared verses): joy and peace are frequently paired (Gal 5:22 — adjacent Spirit-fruits; Isa 55:12 — "go out in joy... led forth in peace"). Joy is the positive affective response; peace is the positive existential condition. Joy can be present without complete peace (James 1:2 — "count it all joy... when you meet trials of various kinds" — joy in trials doesn't require peaceful resolution); peace at its fullest produces joy but is not joy itself. | OBS-117-T2-129 | OBSERVATION | R117 peace | Isa 26:3, Gal 5:22 | The most precisely delineable boundary is peace vs. trust (R163): trust is the inner act of reliance; peace is the condition trust produces (Isa 26:3 — trust → peace; Psa 4:8 — trust posture → peace condition). They are causally ordered: trust precedes and produces peace; peace confirms and deepens trust. The boundary: trust is volitional (an act), peace is resultant (a state). They are not interchangeable. The peace-joy boundary: peace is the ground-condition; joy is the response-condition. Joy arises from peace but is the felt affective expression, not the existential ground. The peace-rest boundary remains partially unclear — these two may be dimensions of a single complex rather than distinct characteristics. Deferred to Session D. |

Peace vs. joy: kind — they are different kinds of inner reality (existential ground vs. affective response). Peace vs. rest: degree or dimension — the distinction is unclear; rest may be a dimension of peace (a component) rather than a distinct kind. Full answer requires Session D. |

| OBS-117-T2-135 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Isa 26:3, Num 6:26 | Primary terms: (1) Hebrew: sha.lom (H7965G and sub-forms) — root SHA.L/SHA.L.M, meaning completeness, soundness, wholeness; from sha.lem (to be complete, to be at peace, to be paid in full); the root encodes the idea of nothing lacking, everything in its proper place. This is the most semantically significant root etymology in the registry — shalom is not "peace" in the modern reductive sense but "wholeness-completeness-settlement." The root's appearance in sha.lam (to pay, to make restitution, H7999) confirms: shalom is what exists when every debt is settled, every breach is healed, every deficiency is supplied. (2) Greek: eirēnē (G1515) — root uncertain, possibly from eirō (to join) — the condition of things joined together; in the LXX, eirēnē becomes the translation of shalom, absorbing its full semantic range. NT eirēnē is not a Greek concept Hellenised but a Hebrew concept translated. OBS-117-009, Q&A-013. |
| OBS-117-T2-136 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Psa 120:7, Isa 9:6, Psa 37:37 | Sha.lom (H7965G) functions as: (1) Noun (primary): the condition of peace — "in peace I will lie down" (Psa 4:8), "covenant of peace" (Isa 54:10); (2) Noun used as greeting formula: "Peace be to you" (1Sa 25:6, H7965K); (3) Noun functioning as an identity statement: "I am for peace" (Psa 120:7 — ani sha.lom, where sha.lom is the predicate nominative); (4) Noun used adverbially (H7965L): "wholly" (Jer 13:19 — all-verses-fail, no inner-being); (5) Noun in construct chain as descriptor: "covenant of peace" (brit sha.lom), "man of peace" (ish sha.lom, Psa 37:37), "Prince of Peace" (sar sha.lom, Isa 9:6). The identity-statement form (ani sha.lom — "I am peace") is analytically distinctive: sha.lom becomes a predicate nominative, identifying the person with the characteristic. This grammatical form is unique in the registry and is the most concentrated expression of the moral-character dimension. |
| OBS-117-T2-137 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Psa 38:3, Isa 9:7, Mic 5:5, Judg 6:24 | Sha.lom's semantic range is the widest of any single term in the programme's vocabulary: (1) Completeness/soundness (Psa 38:3 — "no sha.lom in my bones"); (2) Safety/welfare (Jer 29:11 — "plans for sha.lom"); (3) Peace as inner condition (Psa 4:8 — "in sha.lom I will lie down"); (4) Peace as relational condition (Num 25:12 — "covenant of sha.lom"); (5) Peace as eschatological reality (Isa 9:7 — "no end to his sha.lom"); (6) Peace as messianic identity (Mic 5:5 — "he shall be their sha.lom"); (7) Welfare/prosperity (H7965I — prosperity of the wicked, Psa 73:3); (8) Greeting (H7965K — peace-blessing in greeting, 1Sa 25:6); (9) Friendship (H7965J — bond of close friendship, 1Ch 12:17); (10) Divine identity (H7965H — YHWH-Shalom, Judg 6:24). | OBS-117-T2-139 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Isa 48:22, Isa 57:20 | The vocabulary does not include a single dedicated antonym term, but several terms name peace's absence or corruption: (1) The "no sha.lom for the wicked" formula (Isa 48:22, 57:21) — sha.lom with negation particle; (2) The restlessness vocabulary: balah (to be disturbed/troubled), ra.ga (H7280A — to stir up/disturb) — these name the opposite condition; (3) Isa 57:20 — "like the tossing sea, for it cannot be quiet (sha.qat)" — inability to be quiet as the functional opposite of shaqat-peace; (4) The false-shalom pattern: the vocabulary distinguishes false sha.lom (the words of the false prophets, Jer 6:14) from genuine sha.lom, but the same word is used — the opposition is moral, not lexical. The absence of a dedicated antonym term is itself analytically significant: the biblical vocabulary defines peace positively (through its presence and character) rather than negatively (through a named opposite). Peace is not primarily defined by what it is not. |

| OBS-117-T2-141 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Psa 34:14, Eze 7:25, Jer 29:7 | Yes: (1) Psa 34:14 — "seek peace (sha.lom) and pursue it" — the imperative "seek" (baqash) applied to sha.lom; seeking-peace as a commanded act; (2) Eze 7:25 — "when anguish comes they will seek (baqash) peace, but there shall be none" — desperate seeking of sha.lom; (3) Jer 8:15, 14:19 — "we looked for peace" — qavah (to wait/hope for) applied to sha.lom; the seeking-of-peace-that-does-not-come; (4) Jer 29:7 — "seek (darash) the welfare" — darash (to seek/inquire) applied to the sha.lom of the city. | OBS-117-T2-142 | OBSERVATION | R117 peace | Num 6:26, Phili 4:7 | The LXX's consistent rendering of sha.lom with eirēnē is the foundational datum: the translation decision imports sha.lom's full semantic range into the Greek word, enabling the NT to use eirēnē with full shalom resonance. | OBS-117-T2-143 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Col 1:20 | Yes — G1517 eirēnopoieō (to make peace, Col 1:20) is attested primarily in the NT context and represents a verbal neologism: combining eirēnē (peace) with poieō (to make/do) to create a term for the specific act of cosmic peace-making. This term is thin (1 active verse in the corpus, Col 1:20) but carries maximum theological weight — it names what Christ accomplished at the cross as a unique act. G1518 eirēnopoios (peacemaker, Mat 5:9) similarly compounds the root to create a person-type term. The NT coinage of these compound terms reflects the NT's need to describe both the act (eirēnopoieō) and the agent (eirēnopoios) of peace-making — concepts that the OT vocabulary named differently (sha.lom as noun, baqash sha.lom as seeking-peace). The new terminology reflects new theological content: the specific Christological act of making peace through the cross. |
| OBS-117-T2-144 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Judg 6:24, Isa 54:10, Psa 4:8, Isa 9:6, Col 1:20 | The full vocabulary arc — from SHA.L (root: completeness) through the shalom sub-forms (G/H/I/J/K/L) through the Hebrew silence/rest vocabulary (H5117/H5183A/H8252 etc.) through the Greek eirēnē family (G1515, G1514, G1516, G1517) — reveals that peace encompasses: (1) The root: completeness-as-ground; (2) The inner condition: wholeness, welfare, rest, quiet; (3) The relational form: friendship, greeting-blessing, covenant; (4) The divine identity: YHWH-Shalom, Prince of Peace; (5) The act: making peace (eirēnopoieō), being at peace (eirēneuō), pursuing peace (baqash sha.lom); (6) The person: man of peace, the peaceable (eirēnikos); (7) The eschatological telos: no end to his peace, eternal covenant. | OBS-117-T2-145 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Isa 26:3, Psa 4:8, Lam 3:17 | Three candidate primary verses — the instruction notes that T7.2.5 will designate the single primary anchor. | OBS-117-T2-146 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Isa 9:6, Psa 4:8, Jam 3:17, Eph 2:14, Num 6:26 | The peace registry draws from all major biblical literary forms: (1) Prophecy (dominant in OT): Isa 9:6/7, 26:3, 30:15, 53:5, 54:10, Jer 29:11, Mic 5:5, Eze 34:25, 37:26 — the prophetic corpus carries the eschatological and messianic peace evidence and the refusal-of-peace pattern; (2) Psalms: Psa 4:8, 28:3, 32:3, 34:14, 62:5, 85:8, 119:165, 120:7, 131:2 — carries the experiential/devotional inner-peace evidence; (3) Wisdom: Pro 3:2/17, Ecc 9:17, Jam 3:17/18 — carries the peace-wisdom correlation; (4) Epistle: Rom 5:1, 8:6, Phili 4:7, Gal 5:22, 1Th 5:23, Col 1:20, Eph 2:14 — carries the NT theological synthesis; (5) Torah/Narrative: Num 6:26, Deu 28:65, Deu 29:19, Gen 28:21 — foundational covenant structures; (6) Lament: Lam 3:17 — the peaceless-soul at depth.
